(2) Quote→claim support: the supporting quote ("compared with symmetrical logos, asymmetrical logos tend to be more arousing, leading to increased perceptions of excitement") matches the paper's abstract near-verbatim, and the paper establishes arousal as the MEDIATOR (subjective arousal mediates asymmetric-exposure → perceived brand excitement). The apparent "counter" (PLOS One "Beauty of symmetry," 2025) finds symmetrical logos raise perceived PRODUCT QUALITY via perceived STABILITY — a DIFFERENT outcome construct, and actually complementary (symmetry→stability→quality; asymmetry→arousal→excitement), not a refutation. Caveat for synthesis only (does not affect this claim's truth): the brand-EQUITY benefit of asymmetry is conditional on an exciting brand personality and can HURT non-exciting brands — that boundary belongs on any equity claim, but is outside the scope of THIS arousal-excitement claim.

No source disputing the asymmetry→arousal→excitement direction was found.

#### V31 `ab3a09d9` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/002224299806200202 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "High-recognition logos (accurate recognition created by high investment) should be very natural, very harmonious, and moderately elaborate."

*Evidence:* CLAIM STANDS. The supporting quote is reproduced verbatim from Henderson & Cote (1998), "Guidelines for Selecting or Modifying Logos," Journal of Marketing 62(2), DOI 10.1177/002224299806200202: "High-recognition logos (accurate recognition created by high investment) should be very natural, very harmonious, and moderately elaborate." Confirmed by two independent web searches, both tying "moderately elaborate" specifically to the high-recognition objective (their other objectives get different profiles: low-investment = less natural/very harmonious; high-image = moderately natural/elaborate), so it is not a misread of a different cell. The moderate optimum is mechanistically grounded: naturalness DECREASES false recognition while elaborateness INCREASES false recognition, so the net optimum for "accurate" (correct-minus-false) recognition is moderate, not maximal — an inverted-U. Thus the inference that this "falsifies more-elaboration-is-always-better for recognition" is valid and correctly scoped to the paper's own "accurate recognition" construct. Source quality is excellent: top-tier peer-reviewed Journal of Marketing, 195-logo empirical study on 13 design characteristics, foundational (~3,000+ citations). Not outdated in a disqualifying way (validated cross-nationally by van der Lans, Cote, Henderson et al. 2009 and replicated in Chile 2020). Not marketing fluff. Sole interpretive element is the word "falsifies," which is the claim author's framing but a fair reading of a prescription that is moderate rather than maximal. NOTE: direct PDF access was blocked (SAGE returned HTTP 403; uwinnipeg mirror had a TLS cert mismatch), so verbatim confirmation rests on two independent search syntheses plus the well-established framework rather than my own reading of the full paper.
*Counter-source:* A targeted search for disconfirming evidence (Henderson Cote elaborateness false/correct recognition inverted-U) found no credible source disputing the moderate-elaborateness prescription; cited follow-ups (van der Lans et al. 2009 Marketing Science cross-national; 2020 Chilean replication in Estudios de Administracion) extend rather than contradict it.

Every claim component maps: (a) "three studies" — confirmed; (b) description "parts of the characters in the company name are intentionally missing/blanked out" — near-verbatim from the abstract; (c) trustworthiness LOWERED — "unfavorable influence on perceived firm trustworthiness"; (d) innovativeness RAISED — "favorable influence on perceived firm innovativeness." Mechanisms also confirmed (trust effect mediated by perceived clarity; innovativeness by perceived interestingness). Source quality (top-tier FT50 peer-reviewed Journal of Marketing) matches the claim strength. No contradicting or replication-failure evidence found; later work (regulatory-focus prevention/promotion moderator; logo-frame studies) extends and is consistent with the finding. The "trade-off" framing is accurate and appropriately hedged — the claim correctly isolates the two-dimensional trust-vs-innovativeness trade-off and does not overstate it as a net-negative effect. Not outdated in any disqualifying sense (foundational result, still cited/built upon in 2025). I attempted refutation on all five checklist fronts (quote overreach, contradicting evidence, source quality, recency, marketing fluff) and found no grounds.

This is the exact supporting quote, word-for-word. Searched specifically for contradicting evidence (Pieters & Wedel complexity work, van Grinsven & Das complexity-moderates-exposure); these add elaborateness/complexity nuance but do not contradict the naturalness reversal. Source quality (A-tier primary, cross-nationally validated incl. Chile replication) matches or exceeds the claim's strength; not marketing, not outdated for "what the research establishes."
*Counter-source:* Strongest steelman comes from the SAME paper and does not contradict: Henderson & Cote report natural is "universally" beneficial on direct outcomes (positive affect, true recognition, subjective familiarity, shared meaning) and only hurts false recognition — so "more natural wins" on every legitimate brand goal, and the less-natural prescription applies only to the deliberately-deceptive false-familiarity objective. This narrows the scope of the reversal but confirms, not refutes, the carefully-worded claim. No external source disputes the recommendation.

#### V42 `ae3d2725` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The exposure-to-preference effect for brand names and packages operates through processing fluency and its MISATTRIBUTION at judgment time, not through a direct affective response to repetition. This identifies the causal mechanism (fluency, not raw familiarity) that an evidence-based branding skill must invoke when justifying logo/identity design choices."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/24099086_Effects_of_Brand_Logo_Complexity_Repetition_and_Spacing_on_Processing_Fluency_and_Judgment (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "more recent evidence suggests that prior exposure leads to processing fluency at the time of judgment. It is a misattribution about the source of this processing fluency that results in preference for the stimulus."

*Evidence:* The source (Janiszewski & Meyvis 2001, JCR 28(1):18-32) is legitimate and the quote is accurate, but the CLAIM overreaches on its load-bearing assertion: that exposure-to-preference works through fluency-misattribution "NOT through a direct affective response to repetition," and that this is "THE causal mechanism" a skill "MUST invoke." This presents a contested debate as settled and draws a false dichotomy. (1) The mechanism is explicitly documented as unsettled — the mere-exposure literature "remains uncertain, with some positing affective processes and others perceptual/motor fluency." (2) The leading fluency accounts are THEMSELVES affective, refuting the "fluency not affect" split: the two-factor theory is exposure→fluency→increased positive affect, and Winkielman & Cacioppo (2001, JPSP) "Hedonic Fluency Model" shows fluency is "inherently positive and triggers mild positive affect," measured directly via facial EMG — fluency produces real affect, not a cold signal merely misattributed. (3) Zajonc's affective-primacy account (1980; 2001 Current Directions, "Mere Exposure: A Gateway to the Subliminal") directly opposes the claim: affect is "elicited with minimal stimulus input" and mere exposure "cannot be explained by appeal to recognition memory OR perceptual fluency"; subliminal-exposure studies (Kunst-Wilson & Zajonc 1980; Monahan/Murphy/Zajonc 2000) show preference without conscious fluency. (4) The supporting quote hedges ("suggests"), and Janiszewski & Meyvis ran four studies to TEST two-factor vs. dual-process theory — treating the mechanism as an open question, not the definitive, normatively-mandated mechanism the claim asserts. The kernel (fluency influences exposure-based brand preference) is real and codifiable, but the claim's exclusive/definitive/"must invoke" wording is contradicted.
*Counter-source:* Zajonc, R.B. (2001), "Mere Exposure: A Gateway to the Subliminal," Current Directions in Psychological Science 10(6):224-228 (affective-primacy + subliminal exposure, contradicting the exclusive-fluency route); Winkielman, P. & Cacioppo, J.T. (2001), "Mind at ease puts a smile on the face," JPSP 81(6):989-1000 — Hedonic Fluency Model, facial-EMG evidence that fluency produces genuine positive affect, refuting the "fluency-misattribution NOT direct affect" dichotomy; general mere-exposure reviews (Wikipedia/Bornstein 1989) stating the mechanism "remains uncertain."

(Redesign is not free; a brand's most committed customers punish a logo change the hardest.)"

The claim never asserts unconditionality, so this does not refute it, but a codified skill should carry the self-construal caveat alongside the headline finding. Corroborating sources: Walsh, Winterich & Mittal (2010, J. of Product & Brand Management); Pitt dissertation (d-scholarship.pitt.edu/9746); 2020 logo-shape-redesign extension.

The directional main effect is genuine and replicated, so the claim is true as a main effect, but the branding skill MUST encode these moderators rather than asserting "descriptive logos always win."

#### V45 `af5dde1c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The optimal 'natural' level is objective-dependent and can reverse: a logo designed to create a false sense of familiarity and positive affect without heavy media investment should be LESS natural (and very harmonious) — the opposite direction on the natural dimension from the high-recognition prescription. This falsifies a blanket 'more natural/representational logos always win' claim."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/002224299806200202 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Low-investment logos (false sense of knowing and positive affect) should be less natural and very harmonious."

*Evidence:* The claim is an accurate, near-verbatim restatement of Henderson & Cote (1998), "Guidelines for Selecting or Modifying Logos," Journal of Marketing 62(2):14-30 (DOI 10.1177/002224299806200202) — a top-tier peer-reviewed empirical study of 195 logos calibrated on 13 design characteristics. The paper's own published guidelines, reproduced consistently across multiple independent academic citations: HIGH-RECOGNITION logos (high true recognition, low false recognition, high affect) should be "very natural, very harmonious, and moderately elaborate"; LOW-INVESTMENT logos ("false sense of knowing and positive affect") should be "less natural and very harmonious." These are opposite directions on the natural dimension, exactly as claimed. The mechanism corroborates and explains the reversal (not coincidence): "Natural designs universally increase positive affect, shared meaning, subjective familiarity, and true recognition and DECREASE false recognition," whereas "designs high in harmony universally increase positive affect, subjective familiarity, and false recognition." Because naturalness actively DECREASES false recognition, a logo that wants to exploit cheap false familiarity should be less natural — internally coherent. The inference that this "falsifies a blanket more-natural-always-wins claim" is sound and is in fact the paper's central thesis (no single best logo; the optimum is objective-dependent). Source quality matches claim strength (premier journal, ~1000+ citations, foundational). No contradicting evidence found; later cross-national work (Chile validation; Evora 2018 naturalness study) validates rather than disputes. Checklist: (1) supported by quote, not overreach; (2) no contradiction located; (3) source strength matches; (4) 1998 but foundational and since-validated, claim is scoped to what the study established; (5) not marketing fluff. Caveat: primary PDF would not open directly (SAGE 403 paywall; cert error on ssingh5.faculty.uwinnipeg.ca; ECONNREFUSED on diva-portal), so verification rests on three differently-phrased search summaries that converge and include the specific counterintuitive mechanism — strong triangulation but not a direct read of the table.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. Searches for contradicting evidence instead returned corroborating academic citations: Cross-National Logo Evaluation Analysis (HKUST/Ralf van der Lans); the Journal of Marketing Management logo-literature review (Tandfonline 2019); the Chile international validation (ResearchGate 343352869); and the Evora cross-national naturalness study (2018) — all reproduce the natural/harmony/false-recognition mechanism and the objective-dependent guidelines.

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/0022243718820548 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "compared with symmetrical logos, asymmetrical logos tend to be more arousing, leading to increased perceptions of excitement."

*Evidence:* Source verified as Luffarelli, Stamatogiannakis & Yang (2019), "The Visual Asymmetry Effect: An Interplay of Logo Design and Brand Personality on Brand Equity," Journal of Marketing Research 56(1) — DOI 10.1177/0022243718820548. The supporting quote is VERBATIM from the published abstract ("compared with symmetrical logos, asymmetrical logos tend to be more arousing, leading to increased perceptions of excitement"). The claim faithfully restates the paper's empirically-tested mediation path (asymmetry -> arousal -> perceived brand excitement), established across 5 studies using experiments plus secondary financial-valuation data. Source quality is top-tier (JMR, FT50/A*, peer-reviewed) and matches the claim's strength. Adversarial searches for replication failure or critique found NONE; instead they surfaced CONVERGENT independent confirmation from a different lab in a different top journal — Bajaj & Bond (2018), "Beyond Beauty: Design Symmetry and Brand Personality," Journal of Consumer Psychology — reporting the same directional effect (asymmetry -> more exciting). Apparent "counter" findings (symmetry -> higher perceived quality; PLOS One 2024; spillover paper) operate on a different dependent variable (valence/fluency/quality), not arousal, so they do not contradict. The claim is also appropriately scoped: it asserts only the arousal/excitement affective effect (the robust, UNMODERATED core), not the broader brand-equity benefit, which the paper shows is moderated by brand-personality fit (asymmetry helps only excitement-positioned brands). Claim is supported, current, well-sourced, and not an overreach. For the skill: codify as [STRONG] with the caveat that the downstream brand-equity payoff is conditional on excitement positioning.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Closest candidates — symmetry -> perceived product quality (PLOS One 2024, journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0317229) and Bajaj & Bond 2018 (JCP) — are convergent, not contradictory: the quality finding is a different dependent variable (valence/fluency), and Bajaj & Bond independently replicate the same asymmetry->excitement direction.

#### V47 `af940f4b` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For accurate brand recognition, the prescribed design profile is 'very natural, very harmonious, and moderately elaborate' — note the elaborateness optimum is MODERATE, not maximal, which falsifies any 'more complexity/elaboration is always better' rule for recognition."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/002224299806200202 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "High-recognition logos (accurate recognition created by high investment) should be very natural, very harmonious, and moderately elaborate."

*Evidence:* Claim verified, not refuted. (1) QUOTE ACCURACY: The sentence "High-recognition logos (accurate recognition created by high investment) should be very natural, very harmonious, and moderately elaborate" is confirmed verbatim by multiple independent sources reproducing Henderson & Cote (1998), Journal of Marketing 62(2):14-30, DOI 10.1177/002224299806200202 — the exact primary source cited. No misquote. (2) INTERPRETATION FAITHFUL: The "moderate not maximal" reading is directly supported by the sentence's own deliberate contrast — "VERY natural, VERY harmonious, and MODERATELY elaborate." The shift from "very" to "moderately" for elaborateness alone is textual proof of a non-maximal optimum. Mechanism documented: elaborateness raises BOTH correct AND false recognition, so for accurate recognition (high correct + low false, the paper's stated objective) moderate is optimal. Henderson, Giese & Cote (2004) confirm: "optimization is especially important for elaborateness since its effects are not positive on all dimensions." (3) SOURCE QUALITY: Top-tier peer-reviewed journal, empirical (195 logos, 13 design characteristics), among the most-cited logo papers; this IS the primary source. (4) NOT OUTDATED/CONTRADICTED: Newer work (van Grinsven & Das 2016, J. Marketing Communications; Pieters, Wedel & Batra 2010) shows complexity effects are exposure-MODERATED and conditional — complex logos recognized faster only after repeated exposure, simple better at single exposure — which reinforces rather than refutes "more is NOT always better." No study establishes a monotonic "more elaborate = always better for recognition" rule. Minor caveat (does not change verdict): the falsification is scoped to ACCURATE recognition (correct minus false); the claim correctly anchors to "accurate brand recognition," so no overreach.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The closest candidates (van Grinsven & Das 2016; Pieters, Wedel & Batra 2010) report exposure-conditional/moderated complexity effects, which support — not contradict — the non-monotonic "moderate optimum" framing. Henderson, Giese & Cote (2004) independently affirm elaborateness needs optimization because its effects are not uniformly positive.

*Claim under review:* "Asymmetrical logos are perceived as more arousing than symmetrical logos, which raises perceptions of brand excitement — establishing logo (a)symmetry as a measurable visual dimension with a directional affective effect."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/0022243718820548 (primary)

*Evidence:* DOI 10.1177/0022243718820548 resolves correctly to Luffarelli, Stamatogiannakis & Yang (2019), "The Visual Asymmetry Effect," Journal of Marketing Research 56(1) — a top-tier (FT50) peer-reviewed source. Evidence base is strong: 5 studies combining controlled experiments with secondary data on real brands' market financial valuations. The claim is conservatively scoped to the affective dimension (asymmetry to arousal to excitement) and does NOT overreach: the paper's contingency (asymmetry boosts brand EQUITY only when congruent with an exciting brand personality) attaches to the equity payoff, not to the excitement-perception link itself, which the paper supports unconditionally — so the claim asserts only what is established. No failed replication or direct critique found. One calibration nuance, not a refutation: the broader aesthetics literature is mixed on symmetry-arousal (some work ties symmetry to high arousal/positive valence via processing fluency), so the directional effect is best labeled as established within logo/brand research rather than a universal perceptual law. Source quality matches claim strength; finding is current (2019).
*Counter-source:* Symmetry-preference/processing-fluency literature (e.g., PLOS One 2020 symmetry-preference work; Reber/Winkielman fluency model) ties symmetry to positive valence and, in some studies, high arousal — a construct-framing tension that qualifies generalization but does not overturn the logo-specific JMR result.

*Counter-source:* Closest counter-evidence is adjacent prior scales — Fournier (1998) "Consumers and Their Brands" Brand Relationship Quality (love/passion, self-connection facets) and Lacoeuilhe (2000) French brand-attachment scale (cited in Journal of Brand Management 2022 review, https://link.springer.com/article/10.1057/s41262-022-00279-5) — but both measure different constructs and neither is a validated emotional-brand-attachment (affection/passion/connection) scale, so they qualify rather than refute the claim.

Source is exactly the top-tier peer-reviewed primary the task requires; foundational (~2,500 citations), not contradicted, not outdated (still the standard affection/passion/connection attachment scale, built on by Park et al. 2010). Only adversarial foothold: the parenthetical gloss "investment = willingness to incur costs/sacrifice for the brand" slightly conflates Rusbult-model "investment" (sunk/accumulated resources) with "willingness to sacrifice"; I could not retrieve the exact item operationalization (full-text PDFs returned 402/403/404). But that is a non-load-bearing parenthetical, consistent with the paper's price-premium-as-financial-sacrifice framing and the task's own listed downstream set. One weak refutation ground, below the 2/3 kill threshold.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Residual uncertainty only on exact operationalization of "investment" (primary full-text PDFs inaccessible: msbfile03.usc.edu 404, Wiley 402, academia.edu/scispace/scite 403), which does not affect the verbatim-confirmed core claim.

#### V58 `a2a64710` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The two AA components (brand-self distance and brand prominence) significantly predict behavioral intentions AND actual brand behaviors (pro-brand behaviors), establishing the model as a predictor of real downstream behavior, not just attitude."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/257474486_Attachment-aversion_AA_model_of_customer-brand_relationships (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The components of the AA relationships model (brand self-distance and brand prominence) significantly predict customers' intentions to engage in pro-brand behaviors."

*Evidence:* Claim verified against the primary source it cites — Park, Eisingerich & Park (2013), "Attachment–aversion (AA) model of customer–brand relationships," Journal of Consumer Psychology 23(2):229-248 (ResearchGate 257474486; also Wiley doi:10.1016/j.jcps.2013.01.002). The verbatim abstract states the paper "empirically tests... the underlying process between the AA Relationships and behavioral intentions AND actual brand behaviors, and customer age as moderating the customer–brand relationships." So "actual brand behaviors" — not just intentions — is explicitly tested. The empirical results were confirmed, not null: "The components of the AA relationships model (brand self-distance and brand prominence) significantly predict customers' intentions to engage in pro-brand behaviors," and "brand attachment–aversion influences customers' pro-brand (e.g., repeat purchase) behaviors," with "strong support for... the AA Relationships model." The two named components match exactly (brand-self distance = valence; brand prominence = salience). The supplied supporting quote was narrower than the claim (mentioning only "intentions"), but the fuller abstract and results summary of the same paper back the "actual brand behaviors" half, so the claim is not an overreach. The "not just attitude" framing is independently grounded in the companion paper Park, MacInnis, Priester, Eisingerich & Iacobucci (2010), Journal of Marketing 74(6):1-17, which demonstrated brand attachment (vs. brand-attitude strength) predicts actual purchase behavior, brand purchase share, and need share. Both are top-tier peer-reviewed sources (JCP, JM); construct, components, and downstream variables all match. One caveat for the synthesis: the model's outcome chain is partly mediated (AA Relationships → intentions → behaviors), so "predict" should be read as confirmed nomological effect, not a claim of two independent direct paths. No contradicting evidence found.

*Evidence:* Quote is genuine and on-point. (e.g., to pay a price premium)"; their measured Study-5 predictive-validity DVs were commitment and investment. So loyalty/WTP-price-premium ARE consequences Thomson attributed to attachment, making the "corroborating Thomson's DVs" framing defensible. The finding is independently confirmed by stronger journal sources (Orth et al. store-evoked-affect: attachment->loyalty+WTP price premium; brand-authenticity->attachment->loyalty+WTP-more+forgiveness studies; Park et al. 2010 on actual purchase/share). No credible source contradicts it; it is a textbook, well-replicated branding finding, not outdated, not marketing fluff. Caveats (quality, not refutation): the SPECIFIC cited source is a weak conference-proceedings extended abstract outside the task's stipulated journal venues, and phrases the link as "related to" (correlational literature summary) rather than original empirical proof of "driving"; Thomson 2005's precisely-MEASURED Study-5 DVs were commitment+investment, with loyalty and WTP being conceptual/later-confirmed consequences. But the claim is hedged ("is reported to drive," "corroborating") and its substance rests on independently strong primary sources, so it survives.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found; all sources corroborate. Sole weakness is source tier: cited source is an AMS conference-proceedings extended abstract (Voss & Jimenez-Arevalo 2014), not a peer-reviewed journal among the stipulated venues, and states the link as "related to" rather than proving "drives."

(2) Second-order structure: the paper's CFA "Model 3 assumes three correlated first-order factors reflecting a second-order EA construct," and summaries describe "Affection, Passion, and Connection as three interrelated components" — exactly matching the claim's "three interrelated first-order factors that load onto a single second-order EA construct." (3-5) Item-to-factor mapping confirmed verbatim across sources: Affection = "affectionate, loved, friendly, peaceful" (4 items); Passion = "passionate, delighted, captivated" (3 items); Connection = "connected, bonded, attached" (3 items); 4+3+3 = 10, arithmetically consistent. The supporting quote accurately reflects the paper. The single contradicting source (a non-peer-reviewed research-methods blog claiming "15 items, 5 each") is self-contradicting — its own item list shows only the 10 items — and is overridden by the primary source; not credible. Minor non-refuting caveat: with exactly 3 first-order factors a "second-order" factor is statistically equivalent to a correlated-3-factor model, but the claim faithfully mirrors the paper's own framing. Source quality (canonical, peer-reviewed, 2575+ citations) matches the claim's strength; a measurement model is not "outdated."

#### V70 `a4163ede` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Emotional brand attachment is operationalized as a validated 10-item scale composed of three interrelated first-order factors that load onto a single second-order EA construct: Affection (items: affectionate, loved, friendly, peaceful), Passion (passionate, delighted, captivated), and Connection (connected, bonded, attached). This is the core definition + measurement model for the construct."

**Source:** https://myscp.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1207/s15327663jcp1501_10 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The finalized 10-item scale reflects three interrelated first order factors labeled Affection, Passion, and Connection that map onto the second-order emotional attachment construct. ... The first factor, labeled Affection, included the items affectionate, loved, friendly, and peaceful. ... A second factor, labeled Passion included the items passionate, delighted, and captivated. ... A third factor labeled Connection included the items connected, bonded, and attached."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully restates its primary source with no overreach. The supporting quote is authentic: an independent WebSearch reproduced the identical structure verbatim — "Thomson, MacInnis, and Park's (2005) three-factor model: (1) affection ('affectionate,' 'loved,' 'friendly,' 'peaceful'), (2) passion ('passionate,' 'delighted,' 'captivated'), (3) connection ('connected,' 'bonded,' 'attached')" = 4+3+3 = exactly 10 items. The "interrelated first-order factors mapping onto a second-order EA construct" structure is independently corroborated ("three first-order factors reflecting a second-order EA construct"). Source is top-tier peer-reviewed (Journal of Consumer Psychology 15(1):77-91), appropriate for the claim's strength, and this is the canonical, still-cited foundational scale (not outdated, not marketing). I actively pursued the one contradicting signal — a teaching blog (researchmethods.imem.nl) claiming "15 items, 5 per subscale" — and it fails as a refutation on two grounds: (a) it is non-peer-reviewed and internally inconsistent, naming only the same 10 items while asserting 15; (b) no peer-reviewed source corroborates 15. A separate "5-item" figure (Emerald RAMJ paper) is explained by a WebSearch result confirming "Malär et al.'s 2011 study adapted and shortened this scale to five items" — a later derivative, not the original. Since the claim is explicitly about the ORIGINAL 2005 measurement model, the 5-item and 15-item figures do not contradict it. Primary text itself was paywalled (Wiley 402, ResearchGate 403, academia.edu 403), so confirmation rests on the verbatim supporting quote plus multiple convergent independent academic descriptions rather than a direct read of the article PDF.
*Counter-source:* researchmethods.imem.nl ("The Emotional Attachment Scale") claims 15 items (5 per subscale) — but it is a non-peer-reviewed teaching blog, internally inconsistent (lists only the 10 named items), and uncorroborated. A "5-item" count appears in secondary papers (Emerald RAMJ 10.1108/ramj-05-2020-0024) but is Malär et al. (2011)'s shortened adaptation, not the original 2005 scale the claim describes.

*Claim under review:* "Emotional attachment is empirically distinct from adjacent marketing constructs: in a Study 5 exploratory factor analysis the EA items (Affection, Passion, Connection) loaded on their own factor, separable from attitude favorability, satisfaction, and involvement -- establishing discriminant validity from those constructs."

*Evidence:* Claim verified against multiple independent secondary sources describing Thomson, MacInnis & Park (2005), JCP 15(1):77-91. (a) Study 5 is correctly identified as the discriminant-validity study: sources consistently describe Studies 1-2 = scale development, Study 3 = internal structure, Study 4 = convergent validity, Study 5 = discriminant + predictive validity. (b) Method confirmed as exploratory factor analysis, not CFA/correlation: one source stated verbatim "Study 5 involved an Exploratory Factor Analysis (Oblique Rotation) examining emotional attachment dimensions and measures of brand attitude, satisfaction, loyalty, and involvement." (c) The adjacent-construct triad matches exactly: a source reproduced "evidence of discriminant validity was obtained in Study 5, where emotional attachment was empirically distinguishable from measures of attitude favorability, satisfaction, and involvement" -- identical to the supporting quote's first sentence, indicating the quote is genuinely from the paper. (d) Sub-dimensions Affection, Passion, Connection confirmed. Adversarial concern addressed: the paper's Study 3 CFA settled on a three-first-order-factor/second-order structure (Model 3) and rejected a single-factor CFA (Model 1); I confirmed the claim's "single EA factor" is NOT a conflation with that rejected model -- Study 5's EFA is a distinct analysis showing EA items group onto their own factor separate from the three other constructs, which is the expected discriminant-validity result and does not contradict the internal three-factor structure. The claim is scoped to discriminant validity, faithfully restates its supporting quote, and the added "exploratory factor analysis" detail is independently corroborated. Source is the foundational primary peer-reviewed paper (2575+ citations).
*Counter-source:* None found. No source disputed or qualified the claim; every secondary source corroborated it, and one reproduced the supporting quote's discriminant-validity sentence verbatim. Wiley primary PDF was paywalled (HTTP 402) and a USC author copy 404'd, but convergent secondary corroboration is strong.

The branding-context gloss (individual=consumer, target=brand) is the correct application.

The claim is narrowly scoped to the definition, so the scale critique is irrelevant; that even critics of the scale accept the definition strengthens the corroboration. "Corroborates the definition" is fair: a second peer-reviewed source adopting the same definition is exactly what establishes it as field-standard, which is all the branding skill needs.

#### V74 `a47c23ce` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Prior to Thomson, MacInnis & Park (2005), no validated scale existed to measure the strength of consumers' emotional attachment to brands; the paper develops and validates such a scale (Studies 1-2), establishing emotional brand attachment as a measurable construct."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S1057740805700954 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "a scale to measure the strength of consumers' emotional attachments to brands has yet to be devised. We develop such a scale in Studies 1 and 2."

*Evidence:* CONFIRMED. The claim faithfully matches the primary source (Thomson, MacInnis & Park 2005, J. Consumer Psychology 15(1):77-91). The paper's own abstract states verbatim: "a scale to measure the strength of consumers' emotional attachments to brands has yet to be devised... [we] developed such a scale in Studies 1 and 2" — exactly the supporting quote. Citation details verified across Wiley, ScienceDirect, ResearchGate, Semantic Scholar. Study structure confirmed: Studies 1-2 develop the scale; Studies 3-5 validate it (internal consistency, convergent, discriminant validity vs. satisfaction/involvement/brand attitudes, and predictive validity for commitment/investment). The three sub-claims all hold: (1) no prior validated emotional-attachment-strength scale — the paper's own peer-reviewed gap statement; (2) scale developed/validated — confirmed; (3) construct established as measurable — confirmed. Source is the primary peer-reviewed paper itself (highest quality for this claim); the claim is historical/foundational so not outdated; not marketing.

TWO NUANCES (qualify but do not refute): (a) Adjacent prior scales existed — Fournier (1998) Brand Relationship Quality (6 facets incl. love/passion, validated by Aaker/Fournier/Brasel 2001, α>0.9) and Lacoeuilhe (2000, French) brand attachment scale. But these measure relationship QUALITY / general attachment, which Thomson et al. explicitly distinguish via Study 5 discriminant validity; the field credits this paper as the origin of the emotional-attachment-strength scale, so the construct-specific "no scale" claim stands. (b) Strictly, full validation spans Studies 1-5, not just 1-2; the "(Studies 1-2)" parenthetical correctly references where development occurs per the quote. Recommend the synthesis say "emotional attachment strength" (not "first brand attachment scale of any kind") to avoid overstating against Fournier/Lacoeuilhe.
*Counter-source:* Fournier (1998) Brand Relationship Quality and Lacoeuilhe (2000) brand attachment scale are adjacent prior measures, but of distinct constructs (relationship quality / general attachment, not emotional-attachment strength); they qualify rather than refute. Verified via myscp.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1207/s15327663jcp1501_10 and link.springer.com/article/10.1057/s41262-022-00279-5

#### V75 `a51592fb` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "[STRONG — construct definition] Park et al. **Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1509/jmkg.74.6.1 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We define brand attachment as the strength of the bond connecting the brand with the self... this bond is exemplified by a rich and accessible memory network or mental representation that involves thoughts and feelings about the brand and the brand's relationship to the self. Two critical factors reflect the conceptual properties of brand attachment: brand-self connection and brand prominence."

*Evidence:* Tried to refute; every substantive component checks out against primary + multiple independent academic sources.

(1) DEFINITION — verbatim match. Park, MacInnis, Priester, Eisingerich & Iacobucci (2010), J. of Marketing 74(6):1-17, DOI 10.1509/jmkg.74.6.1 (correct, resolves to the SAGE/AMA paper). Multiple independent academic sources quote the exact wording the claim uses: "brand attachment [is] the strength of the bond connecting the brand with the self." Confirmed by the authors' own paper copy (odbayasal.wordpress.com hosted JM PDF), the 2018 AMS Review employee-brand-attachment paper (Springer), and a Coventry-hosted peer-reviewed paper — all attributing this phrasing to Park et al. 2010.

(2) TWO FACTORS — confirmed. Independent sources state Park et al. (2010) "develop a scale of brand attachment that formally specifies aspects of brand-self connection and add a second factor of brand prominence in memory," and that two-component scoring has better validity than a unified scale. Matches the claim exactly: brand-self connection + brand prominence.

(3) BRAND PROMINENCE = accessibility/salience — confirmed verbatim. Sources render the paper's definition as: "Brand prominence reflects the salience of the cognitive and affective bond that connects the brand to the self... reflected by the perceived ease and frequency with which brand-related thoughts and feelings are brought to mind." The claim's parenthetical ("the accessibility/salience of brand-related thoughts and feelings") is an accurate compression, not a misread.

CHECKLIST: (a) supported by quote — yes; substantive parts verified against primary source. (b) contradicting evidence — none found; the construct is uncontested and still the standard citation (e.g., the 2022 J. of Brand Management review "Brand attachment: a review and future research" uses it as the canonical definition). (c) source quality — primary peer-reviewed JM paper, matches [STRONG] strength. (d) outdated — no; foundational, still actively cited. (e) marketing fluff — no; academic construct.

CAVEAT (non-refuting): The exact phrase "rich and accessible memory network or mental representation" in the supporting quote could not be re-extracted (the saved PDF uses CID-font encoding that defeated text extraction), but it is consistent with everything verified and the three load-bearing assertions (definition / two factors / prominence=salience) are each independently confirmed without it. Minor labeling note: the task frames the 2010 paper as the "AA-model (attachment-aversion)" strand; AA is technically the related Park/MacInnis/Priester attachment-aversion model and this paper's own later label is "Connection-Prominence Attachment Model (CPAM)." That is the task author's framing choice, not a factual error in what the claim says Park et al. 2010 defines. The definitional content for the skill is correct as stated.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. Nearest nuance: later literature labels this model "CPAM" (Connection-Prominence Attachment Model) rather than "AA-model," but this is terminology, not a contradiction of the definition. Cross-checked: link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s13162-018-0110-6; link.springer.com/article/10.1057/s41262-022-00279-5; journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1509/jmkg.74.6.1.

The published abstract — reproduced near-verbatim across four independent WebSearch index pulls (Wiley/myscp, ScienceDirect, Semantic Scholar, scispace) — states: "[the authors] developed a scale in Studies 1 and 2, validated the scale's internal consistency and dimensional structure in Study 3, examined its convergent validity with respect to four behavioral indicators of attachments in Study 4, and in Study 5 demonstrated discriminant validity, showing that the scale is differentiated from measures of satisfaction, involvement, and brand attitudes." The claim's gloss "not a relabeling of attitude or satisfaction" is the correct meaning of demonstrating discriminant validity. A secondary description of Study 5 adds that EA was "empirically distinguishable from measures of attitude favorability, satisfaction, and involvement" while showing predictive validity (commitment, investment, willingness to pay a price premium) — consistent with the claim. (3) Source quality — foundational top-tier peer-reviewed paper (JCP), ~2,575 citations; exceeds the claim's strength. (4) Not outdated — the distinction has been corroborated for 20 years. (5) Not a marketing/press claim — formal scale-validation study. **Supporting quote:** "Emotional attachment (EA) is defined as the emotional bond connecting an individual with a specific target (Thomson, MacInnis, and Park 2005)."

*Evidence:* CONFIRMED. The cited Springer chapter (DOI 10.1007/978-3-319-11797-3_10) is "An Alternative Scale of Emotional Attachment" by Kevin E. Voss and Fernando R. Jimenez-Arevalo, in the Proceedings of the 2010 AMS Annual Conference (Springer Cham, 2015; doc dated Oct 2014). Attribution "Jimenez & Voss" is correct (order reversed). The quoted definition is present and attributed to TMP 2005 verbatim: "Emotional attachment (EA) is defined as the emotional bond connecting an individual with a specific target (Thomson, MacInnis, and Park 2005)." (verified via UTEP ScholarWorks repository metadata + two web searches; full PDF paywalled). The same authors published the fuller, fully peer-reviewed journal version: Jimenez & Voss (2014), "An Alternative Approach to the Measurement of Emotional Attachment," Psychology & Marketing (DOI 10.1002/mar.20700) -- a constraint-approved venue -- so the "peer-reviewed source" requirement is satisfied even though the cited link is conference proceedings. The TMP-2005 definition (an emotion-laden bond between person and target; three subscales affection/passion/connection) remains field-standard and is not outdated or contradicted (still used in the 2022 Journal of Brand Management attachment review). TWO CAVEATS for the synthesis: (1) "corroborates" overstates the relationship -- Jimenez & Voss ADOPT and CITE TMP's definition rather than independently validate it; it is consensual (field-standard) corroboration, not empirical confirmation. Notably their paper actually CRITIQUES the TMP measurement SCALE and proposes an alternative, while agreeing on the DEFINITION -- so the claim, scoped to the definition only, holds and is arguably reinforced. (2) Cite the Psychology & Marketing (2014) journal article, not the AMS proceedings chapter, to meet the journal-only constraint cleanly. Recommend softening "corroborates" to "a second peer-reviewed source adopts/endorses the same definition."
*Counter-source:* Jimenez & Voss themselves are partial critics: their paper title is "An Alternative Scale/Approach to the Measurement of Emotional Attachment" -- they dispute TMP 2005's measurement scale while adopting its definition. Also note the cited primary link is AMS conference proceedings (weaker than journal); the journal-grade version is Psychology & Marketing 2014, DOI 10.1002/mar.20700.

#### V82 `a6cadff4` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "[STRONG — conceptual differentiation] Brand attachment is conceptually distinct from brand attitude strength: attachment varies by the STRENGTH of the self-brand bond (not by attitude valence), is largely time-dependent, and reflects a more advanced relationship stage — whereas strong attitudes can form quickly via elaboration and need not be time-dependent. This is the construct-boundary the skill needs to separate attachment from attitude strength."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1509/jmkg.74.6.1 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "What varies is not the valence of the attachment but rather the strength of the bond connecting the brand with the self and its prominence... whereas attachment is largely time-dependent, brand attitude strength need not be."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the full text of the primary source itself (Park, MacInnis, Priester, Eisingerich & Iacobucci 2010, Journal of Marketing 74(6):1-17). I retrieved the article PDF (mirror: odbayasal.wordpress.com), which was obfuscated by a +42 byte font-shift; after reversing the shift, the paper's conceptual-differentiation passage reads VERBATIM:

"...attachments vary in strength from weak to strong. The opposite of strong attachment is weak attachment. What varies is not the valence of the attachment but rather the strength of the bond connecting the brand with the self and its prominence. Finally, whereas attachment is largely time dependent, brand attitude strength need not be... Such self-brand links develop over time... In contrast, strong brand attitudes need not be time dependent. They are based on thoughtful processing (elaboration) and can be formed in a limited time. Because attachments develop over time while strong brand attitudes need not, attachment may reflect a more advanced stage of relationship development."

Every clause of the claim is directly supported, including the two parts I treated adversarially as possible overreach:
(1) "varies by STRENGTH of the bond, not valence" -> verbatim ("What varies is not the valence... but rather the strength of the bond connecting the brand with the self");
(2) "largely time-dependent" -> verbatim ("attachment is largely time dependent");
(3) "more advanced relationship stage" -> near-verbatim ("attachment may reflect a more advanced stage of relationship development");
(4) "strong attitudes can form quickly via elaboration and need not be time-dependent" -> verbatim ("strong brand attitudes need not be time dependent. They are based on thoughtful processing (elaboration) and can be formed in a limited time").

Checklist: (1) Supported by quote — yes, verbatim. (2) Contradicting evidence — none found; the distinction is the paper's empirically discriminant-validated central thesis and the canonical citation, accepted in downstream reviews (e.g., J. Brand Management 2022). (3) Source quality — primary, top-tier peer-reviewed Journal of Marketing (~5000+ citations); exceeds the bar for a [STRONG] conceptual claim. (4) Outdated — no; a foundational construct-definition paper, and construct boundaries do not expire. (5) Marketing fluff — no; peer-reviewed empirical article.

Only nuance: the claim hardens the authors' hedge "attachment MAY reflect a more advanced stage" to "reflects." This is a trivial de-hedging of academic caution, not a material overreach, and the claim is correctly scoped/tagged "[STRONG — conceptual differentiation]." Not grounds for refutation.
*Counter-source:* None found. No credible peer-reviewed source disputes the conceptual or empirical distinction between brand attachment and brand attitude strength; targeted searches for criticism/lack-of-discriminant-validity returned only the source paper and works that build on it.

**Source:** https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/The-Ties-That-Bind:-Measuring-the-Strength-of-to-Thomson-Macinnis/2796a5df2b41448a9720cbd00d0648e2637c17ca (primary)

*Evidence:* CONFIRMED by primary source. Thomson, MacInnis & Park (2005), "The Ties That Bind" (J. Consumer Psychology 15(1):77-91) is a five-study scale-development paper. The supporting quote is near-verbatim from the paper's own abstract and is independently reproduced across multiple sources: SCIRP, Wiley/JCP (myscp.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1207/s15327663jcp1501_10), Semantic Scholar (the cited URL), ScienceDirect, and scispace (which logs 2,575 citations). ONE NUANCE (does not refute): Thomson Study 5 tested discriminant validity from "brand attitudes" (attitude valence/favorability), whereas the precise "attitude STRENGTH" construct is the separate contribution of Park et al. (2010). The claim's phrase "attachment-vs-attitude-strength differentiation" loosely bridges these, but the claim accurately describes what Study 5 actually did and uses "directly supporting" (an evidential-contribution framing, not an identity/misattribution claim). The research question explicitly asks (instruction d) how attachment differs from satisfaction/attitudes, so this is in-scope. The claim is well-supported, current, and from a primary source.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source disputes the finding. The only qualifier is conceptual: "brand attitudes" (Thomson 2005 Study 5) vs "attitude strength" (Park et al. 2010, a separate paper) are distinct constructs, so Thomson Study 5 supports — but does not itself establish — the attachment-vs-attitude-strength differentiation. This refines rather than refutes the claim.

The singular phrasing "as THE L/WOM/R outcome" denotes a single combined DV, which the source does not support — in fact the study's contribution is that brand love separately predicts each of three distinct behavioral outcomes. So the parenthetical is both unsupported by the quote and contradicted by the primary source. Residual hedge: extraction was partial (~45KB of a 16-page paper), but the actual outcome table and scale appendix WERE captured and both show separateness; nothing in the text supports a combined operationalization.

#### V92 `aa0a4204` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Brand attachment has incremental predictive validity OVER brand attitude strength for four real-world dependent variables: intentions to perform difficult (resource-using) behaviors, actual purchase behaviors, brand purchase share, and need share. This positions attachment, not attitude strength, as the stronger driver of consequential brand behavior."

**Source:** https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/Brand-Attachment-and-Brand-Attitude-Strength:-and-Park-Macinnis/a597be324727b93bb86020d022287b8bfa7ee7f8 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "they demonstrate that brand attachment offers value over brand attitude strength in predicting (1) consumers' intentions to perform difficult behaviors (those they regard as using consumer resources), (2) actual purchase behaviors, (3) brand purchase share ... and (4) need share"

*Evidence:* CONFIRMED against the primary source. Park, MacInnis, Priester, Eisingerich & Iacobucci (2010), Journal of Marketing 74(6):1-17 — published abstract states VERBATIM that "brand attachment offers value over brand attitude strength in predicting (1) consumers' intentions to perform difficult behaviors (those they regard as using consumer resources), (2) actual purchase behaviors, (3) brand purchase share ... and (4) need share." Sentence 1 of the claim is a direct paraphrase of this; "offers value over ... in predicting" = "incremental predictive validity over." Verified via two independent search returns (SAGE/SSRN abstract) plus construct-validity detail: CFA shows attachment and attitude strength correlate r=.66 and the perfectly-correlated model is rejected, so the two are empirically distinct and "incremental validity over attitude strength" is meaningful, not a confound. Source is top-tier peer-reviewed (Journal of Marketing, FT50), primary, ~thousands of citations, not outdated for a foundational construct paper, not marketing material. No contradicting/qualifying source found; the only scholarly tension (overlap with attitude strength) is exactly what the paper resolves empirically. Sentence 2 ("positions attachment, not attitude strength, as the stronger driver of consequential brand behavior") is a faithful statement of the paper's thesis, properly scoped to consequential/difficult behavior — the domain where attachment shows incremental dominance; the "not attitude strength" wording is mild rhetorical sharpening but remains comparative, not refutation-grade. Caveat: the in-text beta coefficients could not be re-extracted (PDF body uses a custom-encoded subset font), but the verbatim abstract is the authoritative summary of these exact findings. 0 of 3 refutation grounds met.

**Source:** https://myscp.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1207/s15327663jcp1501_10 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The finalized 10-item scale reflects three interrelated first order factors labeled Affection, Passion, and Connection that map onto the second-order emotional attachment construct. ... "Interrelated"/"correlated" first-order factors loading on a single higher-order EA construct is precisely the accepted model. (4) Validation confirmed: internal consistency, convergent validity (vs. behavioral indicators), discriminant validity (distinct from satisfaction, involvement, brand attitudes), and predictive validity (commitment, investment). Source quality is top-tier (Journal of Consumer Psychology 15(1):77-91, 2575+ citations; the canonical operationalization, still the basis for new 2025 attachment scales). The supporting quote is verbatim-accurate to the paper. #### V95 `aa6c0277` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "[STRONG — conceptual differentiation] Brand attachment is conceptually distinct from brand attitude strength: attachment varies by the STRENGTH of the self-brand bond (not by attitude valence), is largely time-dependent, and reflects a more advanced relationship stage — whereas strong attitudes can form quickly via elaboration and need not be time-dependent. **Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1509/jmkg.74.6.1 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "What varies is not the valence of the attachment but rather the strength of the bond connecting the brand with the self and its prominence... whereas attachment is largely time-dependent, brand attitude strength need not be."

*Evidence:* Claim SURVIVES adversarial review; primary source is genuine and the load-bearing distinctions are verbatim-confirmed.

PRIMARY SOURCE VERIFIED: DOI 10.1509/jmkg.74.6.1 = Park, MacInnis, Priester, Eisingerich & Iacobucci (2010), "Brand Attachment and Brand Attitude Strength," Journal of Marketing 74(6):1-17 — a top-tier (FT50), heavily-cited paper whose entire thesis IS the conceptual + empirical differentiation of these two constructs (discriminant-validity tests; differential prediction of difficult behaviors, actual purchase, brand purchase share / need share). Source quality clearly matches a [STRONG] conceptual-differentiation claim.

The ellipsis in the cited quote merely joins these two distinct differentiators and does not distort meaning. The claim's restatement (attachment varies by self-brand bond strength not valence; attachment time-dependent; attitude strength not necessarily time-dependent) is faithful, not an overreach.

Two ADDED framings in the claim are also grounded, not invented: (i) "strong attitudes can form quickly via elaboration" — the same authors' monograph (Park, MacInnis & Priester, "Brand Attachment: Constructs, Consequences and Causes," Foundations & Trends in Marketing) states attitude strength "is a function of how well the attitude object has been elaborated... Stronger attitudes are better elaborated" (Petty & Cacioppo ELM basis); "form quickly" is a fair complement of the paper's own "need not be [time-dependent]." (ii) "more advanced relationship stage" — corroborated by an independent academic summary (consumergateway.org) quoting the paper that attachment is "more solid and enduring regarding consumer-brand relations than brand attitude" (p.2 definition "the strength of the bond connecting the brand with the self"), and by search summaries noting "attachment develops over time as relationships between the self and the entity evolve."

CHECKLIST 2 (contradiction): Targeted searches for a critique/dispute of THIS specific boundary returned none — only restatements. The distinction is still built upon in the 2022 Journal of Brand Management review (s41262-022-00279-5).

CHECKLIST 3-5: Primary peer-reviewed source (not press release/benchmark/forum). 2010 date is not stale — attachment-construct boundaries are stable and remain standard in current reviews.

Caveat (does not rise to refutation): "form quickly via elaboration" is a reasonable verifier-grade gloss/inference on the paper's "need not be time-dependent," not a direct quotation; and I could not clean-extract the PDF body myself (CID font encoding defeated a pure-Python extractor), so the two verbatim halves rest on independent web retrievals of the paper rather than my own byte-level read. Both checks are mutually consistent, so confidence remains high.
*Counter-source:* None found. Searches for a dispute/qualification of the time-dependence and valence-vs-bond-strength distinction surfaced only restatements; the 2022 Journal of Brand Management review (link.springer.com/article/10.1057/s41262-022-00279-5) extends rather than contests it.

The claim's "L/WOM/R outcome" maps exactly to the paper's "LWOMR"; collapsing repurchase intentions into "brand loyalty" is standard. Source is the top-tier primary (Journal of Marketing); 2012 seminal construct, not superseded on these points by the 2017 practical-scale follow-up. *Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source — the actual published PDF of Thomson, MacInnis & Park (2005), "The Ties That Bind," JCP 15(1):77-91 (authors' USC copy, retrieved via Wayback Machine snapshot 20220708053834; DOI 10.1207/s15327663jcp1501_10). Extracted verbatim from Study 5:

(1) SEM Model 8 (EA + rivals simultaneously): "These results (see Figure 3) show that EA continues to predict loyalty and satisfaction (b=0.23, p<0.01) and willingness to pay a price premium (b=0.26, p<0.01) even when the other antecedents are included in the analysis. (2) Conservative hierarchical regressions (attitude favorability, satisfaction, involvement entered FIRST; EA second; DVs = brand loyalty and WTP premium): "The results for brand loyalty showed that EA remained significant even when the variance in these other factors was accounted for first (b=0.14, p<0.02)." Summary: "The measure adds explanatory power to the prediction of these factors even when the variance in attitudes, involvement, and satisfaction is accounted for first." And: "it had predictive power beyond that explained by brand attitudes, satisfaction, and involvement." — confirms the claim's "incremental power beyond" element for BOTH loyalty and WTP premium.

CHASED THE ONE APPARENT CONTRADICTION: a search summarizer asserted the authors "do not propose EA is the only/best predictor." I located the actual passage — a caveat scoped to LOYALTY, not WTP premium: "although our results suggest that EA predicts brand loyalty, we do not wish to suggest that emotional attachment is the only driver of loyalty or that loyalty requires emotional attachment." This is conceptual humility about loyalty; it does NOT retract the empirical "only significant predictor of willingness to pay a premium" result. The claim correctly scopes "only" to WTP premium and to "the model that included those rival predictors" (= Model 8). No genuine contradiction.

Source quality matches claim strength: foundational peer-reviewed JCP paper (2575+ citations), hypothesis-tested via SEM + conservative hierarchical regression, N=179 nonstudent sample. Not outdated (canonical measurement paper; Park et al. 2010 extended it, did not refute). Not marketing fluff. Minor precision note for synthesis (not a refutation): the SEM loyalty path was a combined "loyalty and satisfaction" factor; the standalone "brand loyalty" incremental result (b=0.14) comes from the hierarchical regression — so both halves of the claim are supported, just via two complementary analyses. [STRONG] finding.
*Counter-source:* No credible peer-reviewed source contradicts the claim. The sole apparent contradiction (a web-search summary saying the authors "do not propose EA is the only/best predictor") was traced to the paper's own loyalty-scoped caveat ("we do not wish to suggest that emotional attachment is the only driver of loyalty"), which is consistent with — not contrary to — the WTP-premium "only significant predictor" finding. Primary source: https://myscp.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1207/s15327663jcp1501_10 (verified via authors' USC PDF on Wayback Machine).

#### V106 `ac8b9e05` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Brand attachment has incremental predictive validity OVER brand attitude strength for four real-world dependent variables: intentions to perform difficult (resource-using) behaviors, actual purchase behaviors, brand purchase share, and need share. This positions attachment, not attitude strength, as the stronger driver of consequential brand behavior."

**Source:** https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/Brand-Attachment-and-Brand-Attitude-Strength:-and-Park-Macinnis/a597be324727b93bb86020d022287b8bfa7ee7f8 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "they demonstrate that brand attachment offers value over brand attitude strength in predicting (1) consumers' intentions to perform difficult behaviors (those they regard as using consumer resources), (2) actual purchase behaviors, (3) brand purchase share ... and (4) need share"

*Evidence:* SUPPORTED by the primary source, with verbatim confirmation. Park, MacInnis, Priester, Eisingerich & Iacobucci (2010), J. of Marketing 74(6):1-17. The abstract (confirmed independently via SAGE listing and the SSRN/Semantic Scholar record) states brand attachment "offers value over brand attitude strength in predicting consumers' intentions to perform difficult behaviors (those they regard as using consumer resources), actual purchase behaviors, brand purchase share, and need share." All FOUR dependent variables in the claim match the source exactly; "offers value over ... in predicting" is precisely "incremental predictive validity over." This is strong support for Part A of the claim.

Part B ("attachment, not attitude strength, as the stronger driver of CONSEQUENTIAL brand behavior") is a faithful, appropriately-scoped paraphrase, not an overreach. The paper's measured nuance — surfaced in search: "brand attitude strength was equally strong a predictor as brand attachment for brand behaviors regarded as LEAST difficult to perform" — does NOT contradict the claim. It is the mechanism that makes it true: attachment's advantage grows precisely as behaviors become more resource-using/consequential. The claim's first sentence ("incremental ... OVER") keeps it from implying attitude strength is irrelevant. The paper's own framing ("heart and mind share" vs attitude's "mind share" only) positions attachment as the stronger driver of resource-demanding behavior.

ADVERSARIAL CHECK: An explicit search for replication failures / "overstated" critiques of this finding returned NONE. Existing critiques (Schmitt 2013; Fournier & Alvarez 2013 JCP commentaries; "dark side of brand attachment" work; 2022 J. of Brand Management review) target only the negative/aversion side of the later AA model and conceptual heterogeneity in follow-on studies — not the attachment-vs-attitude-strength result, which the 2022 review still treats as foundational.

SOURCE QUALITY: Top-tier peer-reviewed primary source (J. of Marketing), authored by the construct's originators, ~5,000+ citations. Matches the claim's strength; not marketing fluff, not outdated. No grounds for refutation.
*Counter-source:* None found. Search for contradicting/replication-failure evidence surfaced only critiques of the later AA model's negative-relationship (brand aversion) scope (Schmitt 2013; Fournier & Alvarez 2013, J. of Consumer Psychology) and conceptual heterogeneity in follow-on studies (Journal of Brand Management 2022 review) — none disputes the attachment-vs-attitude-strength predictive finding under review.

#### V108 `acce4742` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "[STRONG — conceptual differentiation] Brand attachment is conceptually distinct from brand attitude strength: attachment varies by the STRENGTH of the self-brand bond (not by attitude valence), is largely time-dependent, and reflects a more advanced relationship stage — whereas strong attitudes can form quickly via elaboration and need not be time-dependent. **Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1509/jmkg.74.6.1 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "What varies is not the valence of the attachment but rather the strength of the bond connecting the brand with the self and its prominence... whereas attachment is largely time-dependent, brand attitude strength need not be."

*Evidence:* Claim VERIFIED against the primary source and corroborated; could not be refuted. (1) The supporting quote is verbatim in the actual JM 2010 article: the search index of co-author Priester's USC-hosted published PDF (msbfile03.usc.edu/digitalmeasures/priester/intellcont/jm-1.pdf) returns word-for-word "What varies is not the valence of the attachment but rather the strength of the bond connecting the brand with the self and its prominence" and "whereas attachment is largely time dependent, brand attitude strength need not be." This directly supports the two load-bearing assertions (varies by bond-strength not valence; attachment time-dependent vs. attitude strength not). (2) The two paraphrased sub-claims are corroborated by the same authors' companion monograph (Park, MacInnis & Priester, "Brand Attachment: Constructs, Consequences, and Causes," Foundations & Trends in Marketing), which I downloaded and extracted in full: attitude strength "is a function of how well the attitude object has been elaborated (Petty and Cacioppo, 1986)" — elaboration-driven, supporting "strong attitudes can form quickly via elaboration"; and attachment "accounts for higher-order behaviors associated with commitment to a relationship" developing "over time" — supporting "more advanced relationship stage." (3) Adversarial search for refutation ("not distinct / same construct / multicollinearity") found the opposite: constructs are "related yet empirically distinct... cannot be confounded." The known empirical-discriminant-validity debate (moderate-high correlation between the two) concerns statistical separability, NOT the conceptual boundary the claim draws, and the claim is correctly scoped to CONCEPTUAL differentiation. (4) Source = Park et al. (2010), Journal of Marketing 74(6):1-17 — flagship peer-reviewed journal, the exact right primary source for a conceptual-differentiation claim, still the standard reference (2022 Journal of Brand Management review builds on it). Not outdated, not marketing fluff. Minor honest note for the skill: "more advanced relationship stage" is a reasonable paraphrase (developed over time, commitment-level behaviors), not a verbatim phrase; and an empirical-separability caveat exists in the literature but does not touch the conceptual claim made here.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. Adversarial searches for empirical non-distinctness surfaced only confirmations ("related yet empirically distinct... cannot be confounded"). The sole adjacent caveat is the general discriminant-validity debate (attachment vs. attitude strength correlate moderate-to-high), but it addresses statistical separability, not the conceptual boundary in the claim, and does not contradict it.

#### V112 `af4bc31e` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "The AA Relationships measure is empirically DISTINCT from, and outperforms, three adjacent constructs tested side-by-side: brand attachment, emotional valence, and brand attitude strength, demonstrated across a set of dependent variables. This directly substantiates the research question's 'attachment vs attitude strength as distinct predictors' theme. [STRONG]."

**Source:** https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=2439836 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "key distinguishing differences between the AA Relationships measure and other alternative relationship measures (i.e., brand attachment, emotional valence and brand attitude strength) based on a set of dependent variables"

*Evidence:* OVERREACH / SOURCE-THEME MISATTRIBUTION (not a fabrication, but the claim as worded is unsupported).

1) SOURCE IDENTITY: SSRN abstract_id=2439836 is Park, Eisingerich & Park (2013), "Attachment-Aversion (AA) Model of Customer-Brand Relationships," J. Consumer Psychology 23(2). This is NOT the research question's source #2. Source #2 (Park, MacInnis, Priester, Eisingerich & Iacobucci 2010, "Brand Attachment and Brand Attitude Strength," J. Marketing 74(6):1-17) is a DIFFERENT paper = SSRN abstract_id=1605782. The "attachment vs attitude strength as distinct predictors" theme is the 2010 paper's contribution (it showed brand attachment beats brand attitude strength at predicting difficult behaviors, actual purchase, brand purchase share, need share). The claim cites the wrong paper for that theme.

2) CONSTRUCT CONFLATION (decisive): The cited 2013 abstract distinguishes the AA Relationships measure (brand-self distance + brand prominence) FROM "brand attachment, emotional valence and brand attitude strength," which it labels as ALTERNATIVE relationship measures. So in this paper AA Relationships != brand attachment. The claim then says this "directly substantiates 'attachment vs attitude strength as distinct predictors'" — which requires treating AA Relationships AS attachment, while the same paper lists brand attachment as a separate construct AA Relationships is distinct from. Internally incoherent; the paper cannot "directly" substantiate attachment-vs-attitude-strength because its focal construct is explicitly NOT attachment.

3) "OUTPERFORMS" beyond the quote: the supporting quote says "key distinguishing DIFFERENCES ... based on a set of dependent variables" — differentiation, not superiority. The abstract's "strong support for the unique and important contribution" leans supportive but does not establish the side-by-side "outperforms across DVs" wording.

Net: the broad idea (attachment-family construct distinct from / superior to attitude strength) is genuinely STRONG, but it is established by the 2010 JM paper (1605782), not the cited 2013 AA paper. The claim collapses two distinct findings, mislabels the construct, cites the wrong paper for the theme, and adds "directly"/"outperforms" that the quote does not carry. For synthesis, the 2013 paper is at best ADJACENT supporting evidence, not direct substantiation of the named theme.
*Counter-source:* Correct source for the "attachment vs attitude strength as distinct predictors" theme: Park, MacInnis, Priester, Eisingerich & Iacobucci (2010), J. Marketing 74(6):1-17, SSRN abstract_id=1605782 (https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=1605782 ; SAGE https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1509/jmkg.74.6.1). Cited paper (2439836) abstract confirming AA Relationships is distinguished FROM brand attachment/emotional valence/attitude strength: https://myscp.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1016/j.jcps.2013.01.002

[STRONG] label is appropriate: this is the central, SEM-validated conceptual contribution of a top-tier (FT50) paper with 1200+ citations by 2019.
