*Claim under review:* "The AA relationship is empirically distinguished from brand attachment, emotional valence, and brand attitude strength as separate alternative relationship measures, directly supporting the attachment-vs-attitude-strength differentiation central to the research question."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/257474486_Attachment-aversion_AA_model_of_customer-brand_relationships (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "key distinguishing differences between it and other alternative relationship measures (brand attachment, emotional valence and brand attitude strength)"

*Evidence:* PARTIAL OVERREACH on the load-bearing clause. The factual core is true: the 2013 AA paper (Park, Eisingerich & Park, J. Consumer Psychology 23(2):229-248; the ResearchGate pub 257474486 hosts this peer-reviewed paper) does empirically distinguish the "AA Relationship" measure from brand attachment, emotional valence, and brand attitude strength as alternative measures "based on a set of dependent variables," with "strong support for the unique contribution of the AA Relationships model" (confirmed via Wiley/SSRN/ScienceDirect abstracts). So clause (a) is supported by the quote. BUT clause (b) — "directly supporting the attachment-vs-attitude-strength differentiation central to the research question" — is an overreach not contained in the quote. The 2013 paper's contrast is AA-vs-{attachment, valence, attitude-strength}; it validates AA as a distinct NEW construct, treating attachment AND attitude strength TOGETHER as alternatives AA beats. Two constructs both being distinct from a third (AA) does not directly establish they are distinct from EACH OTHER, and the quote makes no pairwise attachment-vs-attitude-strength comparison. The attachment-vs-attitude-strength differentiation "central to the research question" is the DIRECT contribution of a separate paper — Park et al. 2010, J. Marketing 74(6):1-17 (research-question source #2: brand attachment beats attitude strength at predicting actual purchase, brand purchase share, need share). The claim misattributes that direct support to the 2013 AA paper. Source quality is adequate (peer-reviewed JCP), so the refutation rests on overreach/misattribution in the connective clause, not on a bad source.
*Counter-source:* Park, MacInnis, Priester, Eisingerich & Iacobucci (2010), "Brand Attachment and Brand Attitude Strength," Journal of Marketing 74(6):1-17 (journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1509/jmkg.74.6.1) — this is the paper that DIRECTLY differentiates attachment from attitude strength; the 2013 AA paper (myscp.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1016/j.jcps.2013.01.002) instead positions both as alternatives to its own AA construct.

#### V115 `afaa3e39` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Thomson, MacInnis & Park (2005) developed and validated the first scale measuring the STRENGTH of consumers' emotional attachment to brands, establishing emotional brand attachment as an operationalizable, measurable construct (across Studies 1-2, refined through Study 5)."

**Source:** https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/The-Ties-That-Bind:-Measuring-the-Strength-of-to-Thomson-Macinnis/2796a5df2b41448a9720cbd00d0648e2637c17ca (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "a scale to measure the strength of consumers' emotional attachments to brands has yet to be devised. We develop such a scale in Studies 1 and 2."

*Evidence:* Claim VERIFIED across multiple independent academic sources. Paper confirmed: Thomson, MacInnis & Park (2005), "The Ties That Bind: Measuring the Strength of Consumers' Emotional Attachments to Brands," J. Consumer Psychology 15(1):77-91 (citation exact). The supporting quote is genuine and directly supports "first scale": "a scale to measure the strength of consumers' emotional attachments to brands has yet to be devised. We develop such a scale in Studies 1 and 2." The abstract (via SciDirect/Wiley search corroboration) confirms a 5-study program: Studies 1-2 DEVELOP the scale; Study 3 validates internal consistency + dimensional structure; Study 4 convergent validity (four behavioral indicators); Study 5 discriminant validity (differentiated from satisfaction, involvement, brand attitudes) + predictive validity (commitment, investment). So "developed AND validated" and "operationalizable, measurable construct" are fully supported, as are the three confirmed dimensions (affection, passion, connection = EAS). No source disputes primacy; independent peer-reviewed reviews (Springer J. of Brand Management 2022; Emerald RAMJ 2020; ScienceDirect) consistently credit it as the foundational/first emotional brand attachment scale. Source quality (peer-reviewed JCP) matches claim strength; not outdated (cornerstone citation); not marketing. Only quibble: "refined through Study 5" is a loose gloss — the scale is developed in Studies 1-2 and VALIDATED (not literally re-refined) in Studies 3-5 — but the paper does run exactly 5 studies and every core assertion holds, so this is not grounds for refutation.

#### V116 `afadd8d4` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Emotional attachment predicts two managerially important downstream outcomes -- brand loyalty and willingness to pay a price premium -- and does so with incremental (predictive) power beyond brand attitudes, satisfaction, and involvement; in the model that included those rival predictors, EA was the ONLY significant predictor of willingness to pay a price premium."

**Source:** https://myscp.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1207/s15327663jcp1501_10 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "EA is the only significant predictor of a consumer's willingness to pay a premium, underscoring the bottom-line potential to marketers who successfully recognize or create strong consumer-brand attachments. ... In fact, it had predictive power beyond that explained by brand attitudes, satisfaction, and involvement."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the actual paper text (Thomson, MacInnis & Park 2005, JCP 15(1):77-91), retrieved via Wayback archive of the authors' USC-hosted PDF (web.archive.org/web/20220708053834/https://msbfile03.usc.edu/.../published_ea_measure_paper-1.pdf). All three claim components confirmed, the central one VERBATIM:

(1) Two downstream outcomes = brand loyalty + WTP price premium — CONFIRMED, and these are the paper's OWN dependent-variable labels, not a paraphrase of "commitment": "The dependent variables for these hierarchical regressions were brand loyalty and willingness to pay a price premium, respectively"; "its influence on outcomes (brand loyalty and willingness to pay a price premium)."

(2) Incremental predictive power beyond brand attitudes, satisfaction, involvement — CONFIRMED for BOTH outcomes via hierarchical regression entering "attitude favorability, satisfaction, and involvement measures as the first set... followed by EA in the second stage": brand loyalty (EA beta=0.14, p<0.02) and price premium (EA beta=0.25, p<0.01) each "even after accounting for the variance explained by attitude favorability, satisfaction, and involvement." Summary: "The measure adds explanatory power... even when the variance in attitudes, involvement, and satisfaction is accounted for first."

(3) EA the ONLY significant predictor of WTP premium in the model with rivals — CONFIRMED VERBATIM (Model 8 SEM, which is "a model also including measures of attitude, satisfaction, and involvement"): "Furthermore, EA is the only significant predictor of a consumer's willingness to pay a premium, underscoring the bottom-line potential to marketers who successfully recognize or create strong consumer-brand attachments." The claim's supporting quote reproduces this sentence exactly.

The claim is PRECISELY scoped: it limits "ONLY significant predictor" to price premium (not loyalty — for loyalty other predictors were significant/confounded with satisfaction), and ties it to the specific multi-predictor model. No overreach. Source = THE primary peer-reviewed source (JCP), highest possible quality; finding is a timeless historical/scientific report, not a decaying fact. The 2022 Journal of Brand Management review and citation record (2575+ cites; most-used EA scale, 60 studies) treat it as canonical; no contradicting source found.

Minor, non-refuting caveats: paper says "attitude favorability" where claim says "brand attitudes" (same construct, trivial paraphrase); the SEM (Model 8) had mixed/poor fit by the authors' own admission (RFI=0.16), but the incremental-validity conclusion is independently corroborated by the hierarchical regressions, so the substantive claim is unaffected.
*Counter-source:* None. Adversarial searches for critiques/failed replications returned only corroboration; Thomson et al. (2005) EA scale remains the most-used measure in brand-attachment research and is treated as canonical in the 2022 Journal of Brand Management review. The only follow-up (Voss & Jimenez, "An Alternative Scale of Emotional Attachment") refines measurement, not the substantive WTP-premium/loyalty findings.

#### V120 `affc4ca8` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The measured downstream effects of brand love in this paper are exactly three dependent variables: brand loyalty, positive word of mouth, and resistance to negative information about the brand. The brand-love model predicts these better than a single overall summary measure of love. (Note: willingness-to-pay / price premium is NOT an outcome in this study — it is absent from both the abstract and the extracted body text; that outcome belongs to Thomson 2005 / Park 2010.) [STRONG]"

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1509/jm.09.0339 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Both the first-order and higher-order brand love models predict loyalty, word of mouth, and resistance better, and provide a greater understanding, than an overall summary measure of brand love."

*Evidence:* Refuted on the load-bearing parenthetical, which is contradicted by the primary source itself (full text recovered from the Batra, Ahuvia & Bagozzi 2012 PDF, jm.09.0339).

WHAT HOLDS: (a) The three reliably-modeled outcomes are brand loyalty, positive WOM, and resistance to negative information. (b) The abstract quote is genuine and verbatim: brand-love models "predict loyalty, word of mouth, and resistance better... than an overall summary measure of brand love." These parts are correctly [STRONG].

WHAT IS FALSE (the claim's parenthetical): The claim states WTP/price-premium "is NOT an outcome in this study — it is absent from both the abstract and the extracted body text; that outcome belongs to Thomson 2005 / Park 2010." The body text contradicts this in THREE places:
1. Their own conceptual model lists it as a fourth consequence: "consequences of brand love — brand loyalty, positive WOM, resistance to negative information, AND willingness to pay a price premium (see Table)." Followed by "Thus our work builds on prior research..." (i.e., this is Batra et al.'s framework, not prior lit).
2. Measurement section: "We ALSO measured willingness to pay a higher price BUT EXCLUDED IT because of poor reliability."
3. Limitations: "Our candidate measures for willingness to pay higher prices AS AN OUTCOME OF BRAND LOVE were not effective in Study [3], suggesting the need for better measures."

So WTP is (i) a named conceptual consequence of brand love in this very paper and (ii) measured but dropped for poor reliability — it is NOT "absent from the body text," and does not "belong to Thomson/Park" exclusively. The supporting quote also does not support the parenthetical at all (the quote only lists the three modeled outcomes). This is an overreach/misread of a [STRONG] claim. The accurate codification for the skill: conceptual consequences of brand love = loyalty, positive WOM, resistance to negative info, AND willingness to pay a price premium; the WTP measure was excluded for poor reliability, leaving three reliably-modeled outcomes that the brand-love model predicts better than a single summary measure.
*Counter-source:* Primary source full text (Batra, Ahuvia & Bagozzi 2012, "Brand Love," J. Marketing 76(2):1-16), recovered from https://thethingswelove.com/academic-papers/Ahuvia-Brand-Love-JM-2012.pdf (author-hosted copy of the SAGE/AMA article doi:10.1509/jm.09.0339). Passages: prototype-sorting consequence list (4 items incl. willingness to pay a price premium, "see Table"); measurement section ("we also measured willingness to pay a higher price but excluded it because of poor reliability"); limitations section ("candidate measures for willingness to pay higher prices as an outcome of brand love were not effective").

Every load-bearing detail checks out against the JCR page itself: (1) "seven experimental and field studies" — confirmed; (2) "saturation levels from 10%-90%" — confirmed, Study 1 tested nine levels from 10% to 90%; (3) main effect — confirmed verbatim, "low (vs. high) color saturation increases a luxury brand's perceived brand status," mediated by perceived continuity heritage; the supporting quote matches the paper's conclusion exactly. (c) The interpretive sentence "saturation, not just hue, drives a brand outcome" is independently corroborated by Labrecque & Milne 2012 (JAMS, "Exciting Red and Competent Blue": low saturation -> sincerity/sophistication, high -> excitement/competence/ruggedness), so it does not rest on this single paper. Source quality (7 studies, JCR) matches the claim's strength; the work is current (2025/2026), not marketing material. Sole honest caveat (not a refutation): brand-new paper, no independent replication yet of the status finding specifically, and the effect is positioning-moderated — but the claim's "main effect" framing is accurate.
*Counter-source:* No credible source contradicts the saturation->status finding. Studies that appear to oppose it (Labrecque et al. 2025 J. Marketing on color saturation and product efficacy/potency; Zhang et al. 2024 IJTR; "More saturated, better Performance" 2025, Journal of Retailing and Consumer Services) measure a different DV — perceived product performance/efficacy, not brand status — and are complementary rather than contradictory; the JCR paper itself documents the high-saturation advantage for innovation-positioned brands as a boundary condition.

#### V126 `a0fe8e95` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "FIRST documented critique of Aaker (1997): her 'loose' definition of brand personality lets in non-personality, user-imagery/demographic descriptors (e.g., 'feminine,' 'upper class,' 'young') that human Big Five scales deliberately exclude — producing a construct-validity problem (is the scale measuring brand personality, or user imagery?). This means agency claims that an Aaker dimension 'measures' a brand's personality are partly measuring its stereotyped user."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0167811609000172 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "A first criticism pertains to the loose definition of brand personality that embraces several [non-personality aspects] ... This induces a construct validity problem [leaving practit]ioners uncertain of what they have actually measured: the perceived brand personality (a sender aspect) or perceived user [imagery] ... whereas Big Five[scales] exclude gender and social class (McCrae & Costa, 1997), Aaker does include feminine, upper class, young, etc."

*Evidence:* VERBATIM PRIMARY-SOURCE CONFIRMATION. I extracted the actual text of the cited paper (Geuens, Weijters & De Wulf 2009, "A new measure of brand personality," Int. J. Research in Marketing 26(2):97-107 = the cited PII S0167811609000172) from an open PDF and de-kerned it. It reads: "Aaker's scale has recently been criticized on several grounds. A first criticism pertains to the loose definition of brand personality, which embraces several other characteristics (such as age, gender, etc.) besides personality (Azoulay & Kapferer, 2003; Bosnjak... 2007). This induces a construct validity problem and leaves researchers and practitioners uncertain of what they have actually measured: the perceived brand personality (a sender aspect) or perceived user characteristics (receiver aspects). A second criticism concerns..."

This validates every element: (1) "A first criticism" = the paper's own enumeration, so the claim's "FIRST documented critique" maps to the source verbatim; (2) "loose definition" verbatim; (3) "embraces several other characteristics (such as age, gender, etc.) besides personality" = user-imagery/demographic descriptors; (4) "construct validity problem... perceived brand personality (a sender aspect) or perceived user characteristics (receiver aspects)" verbatim, exactly the claim's "is the scale measuring brand personality, or user imagery?"

Independently confirmed via search: Aaker's (1997) 42-item scale DOES contain "feminine" + "upper class" (Sophistication dimension) and "young" (Excitement dimension); the Big Five (McCrae & Costa) excludes such demographic descriptors. The claim's closing inference (an Aaker dimension partly measures the brand's stereotyped user) is a fair, direct application of the sender-vs-receiver construct-validity point. Source quality is top-tier peer-reviewed (IJRM); the critique is still standard and not outdated; it is academic, not marketing fluff.

TWO MINOR CAVEATS (not refuting): (a) "FIRST" should be read as "first criticism enumerated in Geuens et al.," NOT "historically first critique of Aaker." The substantive critique ORIGINATES with Azoulay & Kapferer (2003), whom Geuens et al. explicitly cite for it; reading the claim as historical primacy would be imprecise, but its wording mirrors the paper's "A first criticism." (b) The specific "McCrae & Costa (1997) / feminine, upper class, young" sentence in the supporting quote was not located verbatim in the truncated 'Pages-from' excerpt I could extract — that exact phrasing is the canonical Azoulay & Kapferer (2003) argument. Since both papers are peer-reviewed and both are named in the research question, and the underlying facts are independently true, this is a sourcing-attribution nuance, not a factual error.
*Counter-source:* https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0167811609000172 (Geuens, Weijters & De Wulf 2009, verbatim text confirms claim); origin of the critique: Azoulay & Kapferer 2003, Journal of Brand Management 11(2):143-155, https://link.springer.com/article/10.1057/palgrave.bm.2540162

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported and survives adversarial scrutiny. Primary source = Labrecque & Milne (2013), "To be or not to be different: Exploration of norms and benefits of color differentiation in the marketplace," Marketing Letters 24(2):165-176 (peer-reviewed Springer marketing-science journal). VERBATIM abstract: "is value always gained through differentiation, or is it possible that some value could be forfeited by straying too far from established product category associations?... The results show that while color differentiation is helpful for some product categories, it can also be harmful for others. Specifically, the investigation reveals that adhering to color norms may be beneficial for product categories containing a dominant market leader, especially high-involvement categories." This is a near word-for-word match to the claim. The claim is if anything MORE conservative than the study (it omits the dominant-market-leader / high-involvement moderators that strengthen it). The "refutes the always-beneficial folklore" clause is grounded in the study's own motivating question ("whether visual differentiation is always helpful"). (1) Supported by quote: yes. (2) Contradicting evidence: none found across three searches; adjacent works (Review of Managerial Science 2020 "One color fits all"; Sustainability & Brand Equity 2023) extend rather than contradict; the potential rival Distinctive Brand Assets school (Sharp & Romaniuk, Ehrenberg-Bass) independently agrees color value is conditional, not universal — no source claims distinctive color is always beneficial. (3) Source quality sufficient. (4) 2013 but foundational and reinforced, not outdated. (5) Not press release / benchmark — empirical archival study, 281 brands, 15 categories, 40 subcategories, brand-equity scores. ONE CAVEAT (refinement, not refutation): the study is archival/CORRELATIONAL — color-homogeneity scores cross-referenced with brand-equity scores, not a controlled experiment — so "harms brand performance" is strictly an association; the branding skill should label this an evidence-based archival finding, NOT experimentally demonstrated causation. This nuance does not refute the claim, which faithfully reports the study's own conclusions and "harmful" wording.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Nearest tension — Ehrenberg-Bass Distinctive Brand Assets (Sharp & Romaniuk, distinctivebat.com / marketingscience.info) — actually corroborates the conditional view (color needs Fame AND Uniqueness; category-shared color loses brand-pointing power), so it does not refute the claim. Only methodological caveat: the primary study is correlational/archival, not a controlled experiment.

#### V130 `a1a27091` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Bottomley & Doyle (2006) experimentally show that an appropriately chosen logo colour confers brand meaning immediately and intrinsically, WITHOUT prior conditioning/learning — i.e., colour can add inherent value to a brand name on first exposure. This supports the 'embodied'/perceptually-grounded side of colour meaning rather than purely learned association."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/1470593106061263 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Without prior conditioning, they demonstrate how an appropriately chosen color for a brand name (logo) can bring inherent and immediate value to a brand."

*Evidence:* OVERREACH/MISREAD of the load-bearing conclusion, and it inverts the framework it invokes.

What's TRUE: The Bottomley & Doyle (2006) abstract (Marketing Theory 6(1):63-84) does say "Without prior conditioning, an appropriately chosen color for a brand name (logo) can bring inherent and immediate value to a brand." So the factual base (colour adds immediate brand value without brand-specific conditioning) is real and faithfully quoted.

What's WRONG: The claim's headline — that this "supports the 'embodied'/perceptually-grounded side of colour meaning RATHER THAN purely learned association" — is unsupported and runs backwards to the paper's mechanism and to the Labrecque framework it cites.

1. Mechanism is CONGRUITY, not embodied meaning. Both experiments are context-dependent fit effects (confirmed via Sage/ORCA/Semantic Scholar): Exp 1 = functional products suit "functional" colours, sensory-social products suit "sensory-social" colours; Exp 2 = "when people KNOW how brands are attempting to position themselves, people consider colors congruent with those positions to be more appropriate." Exp 2 explicitly requires LEARNED knowledge of positioning — antithetical to an innate/embodied response.

2. The claim inverts the Labrecque, Patrick & Milne (2013) framework (Psychology & Marketing 30(2):187-202), which it invokes by name. Their definitions: EMBODIED = "emanating from the properties of the color itself... biological response" (red→arousal, long-wavelength→arousal), universal/innate. REFERENTIAL = "context dependent meaning of colors that is LEARNT by individuals over time... semantic associations we learn through contextual cues and life experience" (green→eco-friendly). Functional/sensory-social colour-product congruity + known positioning is the paradigm case of REFERENTIAL (learned, context-dependent) meaning. So Bottomley & Doyle, mapped onto this framework, evidences the LEARNED/referential side — the opposite of what the claim asserts.

3. "Without prior conditioning" means no brand-SPECIFIC classical conditioning is needed (you needn't repeatedly pair THIS brand with a colour), because pre-existing learned colour meanings can be applied immediately via congruity. It is NOT a claim that colour meaning is biological/innate. The claim also slides "inherent value to a brand" (value accruing to the brand) into "inherent [embodied] meaning of the colour."

4. The research question's own design and the existing design-craft branding skill (skills/branding/SKILL.md, line ~275) both file Bottomley & Doyle under CONGRUENCE, not under embodied/referential — corroborating the misclassification.

Correct codifiable finding: Bottomley & Doyle (2006) = colour-product/positioning CONGRUENCE (appropriateness is interactive/relative to functional-vs-sensory-social positioning, not absolute). The embodied-vs-learned question belongs to Labrecque & Milne (2012) / Labrecque et al. (2013), and their framework places congruity on the REFERENTIAL (learned) side. Conflating the two would mislead the skill.
*Counter-source:* Labrecque, Patrick & Milne (2013), "The Marketers' Prismatic Palette," Psychology & Marketing 30(2):187-202 — embodied = biological/innate (red→arousal); referential = learned/context-dependent (green→eco); congruity/appropriateness falls under referential. Cross-checked against the Bottomley & Doyle (2006) abstract and experiment structure (Marketing Theory 6(1):63-84; Sage doi 10.1177/1470593106061263; ORCA Cardiff; Semantic Scholar), whose Exp 2 explicitly relies on consumers KNOWING brand positioning.

*Claim under review:* "Brand POSITIONING moderates colour appropriateness: when consumers know how a brand is attempting to position itself, they judge colours congruent with that intended position as more appropriate. Colour can therefore be used deliberately to enhance/reinforce a brand's desired image, not just match an inherent product category."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/1470593106061263 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "when people know how brands are attempting to position themselves, people consider colors congruent with those positions to be more appropriate"

*Evidence:* CLAIM SURVIVES. Source verified as Bottomley & Doyle (2006), "The interactive effects of colors and products on perceptions of brand logo appropriateness," Marketing Theory 6(1):63-84, DOI 10.1177/1470593106061263 — a peer-reviewed SAGE marketing-science journal (~160 citations), reporting TWO controlled experiments. The supporting quote ("when people know how brands are attempting to position themselves, people consider colors congruent with those positions to be more appropriate") is VERBATIM from the paper's own abstract, independently confirmed across three separate cached-page reads (SAGE abstract page, Semantic Scholar, ResearchGate). The claim is a faithful restatement, NOT an overreach: Experiment 1 establishes product-category congruity (functional colours suit functional products; sensory-social colours suit sensory-social products); Experiment 2 specifically used products classifiable as BOTH functional and sensory-social, manipulated red vs blue, and demonstrated "the ability of color to enhance a brand's desired image" — directly supporting "colour can be deliberately used to reinforce a desired image, not just match an inherent product category." Describing positioning as a "moderator" is the standard framing of this interaction effect (same product, two positionings, appropriate colour flips accordingly). No contradicting evidence found; subsequent congruity literature (color-product premiumness congruency, Springer 2018 product-category colour norms, Labrecque & Milne) reinforces rather than disputes it. Source quality matches the claim's moderate strength; claim is not extraordinary, not folklore, not outdated (foundational and still cited). All five checklist items favour the claim. Caveat: this is a single (if well-cited) paper, so it is best codified as a [STRONG]-but-single-source congruity principle rather than a meta-analytically established law.

#### V132 `a1d9267c` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "Color preference significantly affects brand-logo recall accuracy: logos rendered in well-liked colors were recalled significantly more accurately than the same logos in disliked colors (high-preference > low-preference). This is the qualified, evidence-based version of the color-and-memory claim — the effect is driven by whether a color is LIKED, not by color presence per se."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/23674612_Color_Preference_and_Familiarity_in_Performance_on_Brand_Logo_Recall (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Analyses showed color preference significantly affected recall accuracy. Accuracy for high color preference was significantly greater than that for low preferences."

*Evidence:* PARTIALLY SUPPORTED BUT OVERREACHES. Source is real: Huang, Lin & Chiang (2008), "Color Preference and Familiarity in Performance on Brand Logo Recall," Perceptual and Motor Skills 107(2):587-596 (PubMed 19093619). The abstract verbatim confirms sentence 1: "color preference significantly affected recall accuracy. Accuracy for high color preference was significantly greater than that for low preferences," with the same logos across colors ("four logos shown in six colors"). So the headline finding is accurately quoted. FOUR refutation grounds against the claim AS WRITTEN: (1) OVERREACH — sentence 2 ("the effect is driven by whether a color is LIKED, not by color presence per se") is untested by the study. Huang never manipulated color presence vs. absence; all six conditions were colors varying in PREFERENCE. The study has zero data on "color presence," so it cannot adjudicate the color-presence/recognition folklore it is invoked against — it answers a different question (preference among colors) than the folklore (color vs. monochrome). (2) OMITTED CAVEAT — the abstract explicitly states "the interactive effect of color preference and brand-logo familiarity on accuracy was significant"; the claim presents a clean main effect and drops this. (3) CONFOUND — preferred colors were white/light-blue/black, non-preferred were light-orange/dark-violet/dark-brown; these differ systematically in lightness, saturation, and chromatic-vs-achromatic status (the two highest-contrast achromatics, white and black, sit in the "preferred" set), so recall differences plausibly track contrast/distinctiveness rather than "liking" — causality not isolated. (4) THIN SOURCE for an evidence-based design principle — single 2008 study, key experiment N=48, Taiwanese 18-20yo students only, Perceptual and Motor Skills is Q3 (IF ~1.8, in 2008 an Ammons Scientific high-volume outlet), no replication found. Net: the empirical kernel (liked colors > disliked colors for recall) is genuine and correctly cited, but the claim's interpretive coda makes an inference the study cannot support, omits the significant interaction, and is over-elevated relative to a single confounded, unreplicated, low-tier-journal study.
*Counter-source:* https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19093619/ (own abstract states the significant preference x familiarity interaction and the preferred/non-preferred color sets that confound lightness/contrast with liking); journal tier per SCImago/JCR (Perceptual and Motor Skills, Q3, IF ~1.8)

#### V133 `a1e11149` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "Color preference significantly affects brand-logo recall accuracy: logos rendered in well-liked colors were recalled significantly more accurately than the same logos in disliked colors (high-preference > low-preference). This is the qualified, evidence-based version of the color-and-memory claim — the effect is driven by whether a color is LIKED, not by color presence per se."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/23674612_Color_Preference_and_Familiarity_in_Performance_on_Brand_Logo_Recall (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Analyses showed color preference significantly affected recall accuracy. Accuracy for high color preference was significantly greater than that for low preferences."

*Evidence:* Source verified: Huang, Lin & Chiang (2008), Perceptual and Motor Skills 107(2):587-596. The claim's FIRST sentence is accurately quoted and methodologically fair — Exp.2 used "four logos shown in six colors" (N=48 Taiwanese students 18-20), so the same logos really were rendered in liked vs disliked colors, and high-preference recall (52.1%) did significantly exceed low-preference (26.4%). But the FULL claim overreaches on three grounds. (1) The "driven by liking, NOT color presence per se" clause is unsupported by the design: every stimulus was colored, there was NO grayscale/color-absent condition, so the study varied preference only and literally cannot test "color presence." (2) Confound: preferred colors (white, light blue, black) vs disliked (light orange, dark violet, dark brown) differ systematically in lightness/contrast/chroma, so the recall gap may reflect perceptual salience, not "liking" — the mechanism is not isolated. (3) The abstract states a SIGNIFICANT color-preference × familiarity interaction, so the effect is conditional, not the clean unconditional "driven by whether a color is liked" the claim implies. Source quality is also insufficient for codification as an "evidence-based" principle: a single 2008 study, N=48, one culture-bound sample with culture-specific preference rankings, in a Q3 Ammons-era journal (pre-SAGE Perceptual and Motor Skills, long regarded as low-rigor/high-acceptance), with no independent replication and absent from the canonical color-branding reviews (Labrecque et al.; Bottomley & Doyle). Finding is real and correctly cited, but the claim's interpretive framing and evidentiary weight exceed what the study supports.
*Counter-source:* PubMed abstract of the study itself (https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19093619/) — confirms no color-absent condition, N=48 single-culture sample, and a significant preference×familiarity interaction; journal-tier facts from SCImago/SAGE (Perceptual and Motor Skills = Q3, Ammons Scientific in 2008).

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11002-012-9210-5 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "More specifically, adhering to color norms may be beneficial for product categories containing a dominant market leader, especially high-involvement categories."

(2013), "To be or not to be different: Exploration of norms and benefits of color differentiation in the marketplace," Marketing Letters 24(2):165-176 (DOI 10.1007/s11002-012-9210-5) — same authors as "Exciting Red and Competent Blue" (JAMS 2012); high-quality, on-point source. Abstract (confirmed via RePEc and Springer): "the investigation reveals that adhering to color norms may be beneficial for product categories containing a dominant market leader, especially high-involvement categories." The abstract frames it as a FINDING of the investigation, not future-research speculation. (2) Contradicting evidence — none found; the independent follow-up "One color fits all" (Review of Managerial Science 2020, DOI 10.1007/s11846-018-0325-9) CORROBORATES the direction: color typicality positively relates to purchase intent "particularly in instances of higher perceived risk." The distinctiveness/Ehrenberg-Bass literature emphasizes standing out but does not dispute this conditional finding. (3) Source quality matches a moderate claim. (4) Not outdated — 2013, slow-moving topic, reinforced by 2020 work; canonical study for this proposition. (5) Not marketing fluff — peer-reviewed empirical paper.

The claim drops that hedge ("is specifically beneficial," "strongest," "riskiest"), so it should be tagged [CONVENTION] / single correlational study, NOT [STRONG] causal. I could not access the paywalled full text to confirm whether the dominant-leader x involvement pattern was a formal interaction test vs. an interpretive cross-category pattern; cite it as Labrecque & Milne's reported finding with the correlational caveat. None of these reaches the bar to refute — the claim's propositional content matches the source and is uncontradicted.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Nearest related work corroborates: "One color fits all" (Review of Managerial Science 2020) — color typicality aids purchase intent more under higher perceived risk, consistent with the conformity-benefit-under-high-involvement direction.

#### V135 `a1fd3205` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Color preference significantly affects brand-logo recall accuracy: logos rendered in well-liked colors were recalled significantly more accurately than the same logos in disliked colors (high-preference > low-preference). This is the qualified, evidence-based version of the color-and-memory claim — the effect is driven by whether a color is LIKED, not by color presence per se."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/23674612_Color_Preference_and_Familiarity_in_Performance_on_Brand_Logo_Recall (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Analyses showed color preference significantly affected recall accuracy. Accuracy for high color preference was significantly greater than that for low preferences."

*Evidence:* The study is real and its narrow finding is faithfully quoted — but the claim over-elevates a single, weak, confounded study into "THE qualified, evidence-based version of the color-and-memory claim," and its second sentence asserts something the study never tested. Refutation rests on the verbatim primary abstract (Huang, Lin & Chiang, 2008, Perceptual and Motor Skills 107(2):587-596; PMID 19093619):

1. SOURCE-STRENGTH MISMATCH (the rubric's "low-quality source for a strong claim"). The recall experiment (Exp. 2) had n=48 (27 women, 21 men), one study, no replication surfaced in any search. The journal is a low-impact niche outlet (current IF ~1.4; 2008 was the Ammons-Scientific era). A single n=48 study cannot bear the weight of being the codifiable, evidence-based principle that replaces the "80% recognition" folklore. At most this is a single suggestive study — it must NOT be coded [STRONG].

2. BUILT-IN CONFOUND — preference is perfectly entangled with hue/value/contrast. By design, Exp. 2 reused the colors Exp. 1 ranked: preferred = white, light blue, black; disliked = light orange, dark violet, dark brown. These differ systematically in lightness/value, saturation, and figure-ground contrast — dimensions that independently affect legibility and memory (the very dimensions Labrecque & Milne 2012 and the sibling colour skill emphasize). The design cannot separate "liked colors are recalled better" from "these particular high-value/contrast hues are recalled better." So the causal gloss "driven by whether a color is LIKED" is not isolated.

3. SECOND SENTENCE IS UNTESTED BY THIS STUDY. "the effect is driven by whether a color is LIKED, not by color presence per se" — all 24 stimuli were colored; the study never compared colored vs achromatic/no-color logos. It provides zero evidence on "presence per se." That clause is an interpretive overlay the cited study cannot support.

4. FLAT MAIN EFFECT IGNORES THE INTERACTION. The abstract reports a significant preference x familiarity interaction (secondary summaries: the preference benefit was concentrated in UNFAMILIAR logos). The claim states the main effect unconditionally; the real effect is conditional. Note also: familiarity and sex had NO significant main effect on accuracy.

5. NON-GENERALIZABLE SAMPLE. Taiwanese college students 18-20 (Exp. 1 ~75% women). Color preferences are culturally/demographically variable — "white most preferred" is itself culture-specific — so neither the rankings nor the palette transfer.

Net: the literal sentence-1 finding exists in a real peer-reviewed source (not fabricated, not directly contradicted), but the claim's elevation to an "evidence-based" codifiable principle, plus the untested "not presence per se" extrapolation and the dropped interaction, make it an overreach for the strength asserted. For the synthesis it belongs as a [WEAK / single suggestive study] note with the preference-vs-perceptual-properties confound flagged — not as the evidence-based core.
*Counter-source:* Primary abstract itself, Huang, Lin & Chiang (2008), Perceptual and Motor Skills 107(2):587-596, PMID 19093619 (https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19093619/): documents n=48 recall experiment, the confounded preferred (white/light-blue/black) vs disliked (light-orange/dark-violet/dark-brown) color sets, a significant preference x familiarity interaction, no familiarity or sex main effect, and no color-present-vs-absent comparison — none of which supports elevating it to a strong, codifiable "evidence-based" principle or the "not presence per se" gloss.

#### V136 `a28eba7a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Colour appropriateness is an INTERACTIVE / congruity effect, not an absolute property of a hue: a colour is 'appropriate' only relative to the product type. Functional products are rated more appropriate in functional colours, and sensory-social products in sensory-social colours (Experiment 1). This directly establishes colour-product congruence as the operative mechanism."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/1470593106061263 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Experiment 1 explores the notion of congruity, showing that it is more appropriate for functional products to be presented in functional colors, and sensory-social products in sensory-social colors."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED, claim survives. Source DOI 10.1177/1470593106061263 maps correctly to Bottomley & Doyle (2006), "The interactive effects of colors and products on perceptions of brand logo appropriateness," Marketing Theory 6(1):63-84 — peer-reviewed SAGE journal. The word "interactive" is in the title; a color x product interaction IS the paper's design and central thesis, so "interactive/congruity effect, not an absolute property of a hue" is faithful, not overreach. Three independent summaries (SAGE abstract, ORCA Cardiff repository, ResearchGate/Semantic Scholar) consistently confirm Experiment 1: functional products rated more appropriate in functional colors, sensory-social products in sensory-social colors — the claim restates this almost verbatim. No contradicting evidence found: no replication failures; the category-DIFFERENTIATION literature (Cardamone et al. 2025 Psychology & Marketing; Marketing Letters differentiation work) is complementary (different DV = brand distinctiveness/performance), not contradictory; downstream work ("Evoking premiumness: how color-product congruency influences premium evaluations," J. Retailing & Consumer Services) extends and corroborates the congruency mechanism. The one strong phrase "operative mechanism" is correctly scoped to perceived APPROPRIATENESS (the study's DV is a self-reported appropriateness rating, not preference/sales — the claim never upgrades the DV). Two minor refinements for the merged skill, neither refutation-grade: (1) label the finding as "perceived appropriateness/congruity," not behavioral preference; (2) present the congruence-vs-differentiation tension alongside it, since standing out from category color norms can aid distinctiveness even as it lowers "appropriateness." Source quality (primary peer-reviewed experiment) matches claim strength; finding is still cited in 2025, not outdated. Grade: STRONG.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Differentiation literature (Cardamone et al. 2025, Psychology & Marketing, "Color Inside and Outside the Lines"; Marketing Letters category-color-differentiation work) addresses a DIFFERENT dependent variable (brand distinctiveness/performance vs. perceived appropriateness) and is complementary, not a refutation. Congruency mechanism corroborated by downstream premiumness study (S0950329317302461, ScienceDirect).

#### V138 `a2a1aa1d` — **upheld** · confidence: high

This is a concrete instance of referential (culturally learned) rather than embodied colour meaning."

**Source:** https://academic.oup.com/jcr/advance-article/doi/10.1093/jcr/ucaf029/8120421 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "This effect arises from consumers' association between less saturated colors and the passage of time, leading to perceptions of the brand as having a rich continuity heritage."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED, not refuted. Source: Zhou, Xiao, Yoon & Zhu, "The Color of Status: Color Saturation, Brand Heritage, and Perceived Status of Luxury Brands," Journal of Consumer Research 52(6), April 2026 (DOI 10.1093/jcr/ucaf029) — top-tier peer-reviewed primary source, current. Every falsifiable component checks out: (1) Supporting quote directly matches the published mechanism (desaturated color -> association with passage of time -> perceptions of rich continuity heritage). (2) "Mediation confirmed in Study 3" is ACCURATE — three independent retrievals (two WebFetch passes of the OUP article page + a search snippet) converge that Study 3 establishes the continuity-heritage mediation AND rules out "visual prominence" as an alternative mediator; the chain is saturation -> continuity heritage (mediator) -> perceived status (DV). (3) Referential-vs-embodied framing is supported by the paper's OWN language: a retrieved sentence reads "Color saturation does not uniformly imply the passage of time, as the referential meaning of color is context-dependent," and the association is described as learned "through repeated exposure to products" — matching Labrecque et al. (2013): embodied = innate biological reactions, referential = learned associative/cultural meanings. The paper's heavy context-moderation (mitigated for recent-foundation brands, REVERSED for innovation-positioned brands, saturation meaning healthfulness not age in food packaging) is the diagnostic signature of a learned/referential meaning, not an innate/embodied one — and the paper empirically prefers the learned-heritage route over the more bottom-up visual-prominence route. (4) No contradicting evidence found. Only minor imprecision (not refutation-worthy): "culturally learned" slightly over-narrows an association that is also ecologically/perceptually learned (objects fade with age) plus media-driven; but Labrecque's referential category spans cultural and learned usage, so the core referential-not-embodied classification is correct. Caveat: paywalled full PDF not directly read (token redirect); evidence is convergent abstract/article-page summaries plus search snippets, but convergence across independent sources is strong and consistent.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Searches for disputes/qualifications returned only the paper itself, the supporting Labrecque et al. (2013) "Marketers' Prismatic Palette" framework (Psychology & Marketing), and adjacent saturation studies (e.g., saturation-and-sustainability work) — none contradict the referential mechanism or the Study 3 mediation.

#### V147 `a4a54c54` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The congruency effect REVERSES by product type: for utilitarian (functional/health-framed) products, colour-message INCONGRUENCY raises perceived premiumness — e.g. green on a taste-marketed product or red on a health-marketed product is rated more premium. This is the study's falsifiable, counterintuitive core finding and directly qualifies any blanket 'congruent colour is always better' rule."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S095032931730246X (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "When a product is primarily utilitarian, it will be perceived as more premium when the framing of the product and the color are incongruent"

*Evidence:* Claim VERIFIED against the primary source. Study: Lyons, S.J. & Wien, A.H. (2018), "Evoking premiumness: How color-product congruency influences premium evaluations," Food Quality and Preference, vol. 64, pp. 103-110 (Elsevier, peer-reviewed; two experiments). The abstract, confirmed verbatim across ScienceDirect, Nofima (publication 1511782), and Semantic Scholar (Lyons-Wien), states: "color-product benefit congruency [evokes] premiumness for hedonic products, while color-product benefit incongruence evokes premiumness for utilitarian products." The supporting quote ("When a product is primarily utilitarian, it will be perceived as more premium when the framing of the product and the color are incongruent") is accurate abstract text, NOT an overreach. Direction is correct: green=utilitarian/ecological cue, red=hedonic/arousal cue; for utilitarian products the incongruent pairs (green-on-taste-framed, red-on-health-framed) score MORE premium — matching the claim's examples exactly. This reversal-by-product-type is the documented Study 2 finding, so "falsifiable, counterintuitive core finding" is fair. CHECKLIST: (1) supported by quote — yes, verbatim; (2) contradicting evidence — none surfaced; widely cited, no replication failures or critiques found; (3) source quality — sufficient, a real primary peer-reviewed study attributed honestly to itself, not inflated to a universal law; (4) not outdated (2018); (5) not marketing/cherry-picked — 2 controlled experiments. TWO MINOR CAVEATS that do NOT rise to refutation: (a) the parenthetical "(functional/health-framed)" conflates the product-TYPE moderator (utilitarian vs hedonic) with the message-FRAMING variable (taste vs health) — in the design these are separate manipulations, and a utilitarian product can be taste-framed; the examples themselves correct this, so the actionable core claim is unaffected. (b) It is a single paper (same two authors, food-packaging domain only) without independent replication; the claim attributes it to "the study" and uses it solely to qualify a blanket "congruent is always better" rule, which is a logically valid use of one documented counterexample, not an overgeneralization. Net: the core falsifiable assertion is correct, well-sourced, and current.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Searched for replication failures, critiques, and boundary-condition disputes (Lyons/Wien congruency premium) — none surfaced; related packaging-color work (e.g., PMC10648973 systematic review on packaging color and healthfulness) is consistent, not contradictory. Only caveats are internal: a parenthetical conflates product-type with framing (minor, self-corrected by the claim's own examples), and the finding rests on one unreplicated two-experiment food-packaging paper, so it should be cited as a domain-specific qualifier rather than a universal branding law.

Closest qualifier: the paper's own Study 1 hypotheses were "partially supported" (renaissanceschool.online summary) and the same paper (Study 2) shows saturation/value also drive personality, so hue is not the sole lever — but neither contradicts the red/blue Study-1 thesis.

#### V149 `a5a66dab` — **upheld** · confidence: high

Closest skeptical material — howbrandsarebuilt.com "branding color psychology is mostly bullshit" — targets folklore color-meaning charts, not this peer-reviewed experiment, and actually supports the learned-association/color-in-context view the same Labrecque research program advances; it does not contradict the claim.

Source quality is excellent: Journal of the Academy of Marketing Science (2012), a top peer-reviewed marketing-science journal, ~612 citations — a primary source matching the claim's strength. *Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The only nuance is internal: the paper frames saturation/value as "amplifying" traits with directional (some negative) effects, slightly weaker than the claim's word "independently" — but the underlying empirical finding (all three dimensions significantly shape brand personality) holds.

#### V151 `a61a4406` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "The skepticism triggered by atypical package color flows downstream to depress brand attitude and, in turn, purchase intention (reported path coefficients: skepticism to brand attitude beta = -0.24, p < 0.01; brand attitude to purchase intention beta = 0.54, p < 0.01). The net effect of color-norm deviation on the brand is negative."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11846-018-0325-9 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "atypical package colors implicate negative consequences to the brand"

*Evidence:* Claim is faithful to its peer-reviewed source. Paper verified as Garaus & Halkias (2020), "One color fits all: product category color norms and (a)typical package colors," Review of Managerial Science 14(5):1077-1099. The supporting quote ("atypical package colors implicate negative consequences to the brand") is genuine and verbatim from the abstract, confirmed across three independent sources (Springer, RePEc, ResearchGate). The abstract verbatim establishes the full directional mediation the claim describes: perceived package-color atypicality "increases consumers' skepticism and, contrary to expectations, decreases interest. These affective reactions negatively influence consumers' product attitude which subsequently translates into lower purchase intention." Both mediators run against the brand, so the "net effect is negative" framing is the paper's own conclusion, not an overreach. CAVEATS, not refutations: (1) The two exact path coefficients (skepticism->attitude beta=-0.24; attitude->purchase intention beta=0.54, p<0.01) are behind the paywall and could not be independently verified from open sources; they are plausible and consistent with the reported structural mediation model (attitude->PI ~0.5 is typical), so this is unverified-but-not-contradicted, lowering confidence to medium. (2) The paper's DV is labeled "product attitude," which the claim relabels "brand attitude" — minor, since the abstract itself frames the harm as "to the brand." (3) Generalization caveat for the skill author: a sibling literature (Marketing Letters 2013 color-differentiation; Cardamone et al. 2025 Psychology & Marketing eye-tracking on category color codes; moderate-incongruity tradition) finds deviation from category color norms can HELP under conditions, so this single study's "net negative" must be reported as conditional on an established category color norm, not as a universal law. None of these caveats contradict what Garaus & Halkias actually found.
*Counter-source:* No source contradicts this paper's finding. Nearest opposing evidence (a generalization caveat only): Cardamone et al. 2025, "Color Inside and Outside the Lines," Psychology & Marketing (eye-tracking on conformity to vs. differentiation from category color codes); and the Marketing Letters 2013 color-differentiation study — both show deviating from category color norms can benefit the brand under conditions (e.g., differentiation/attention), so the net-negative result should not be codified as universal. Exact beta values -0.24 and 0.54 unverifiable from open/non-paywalled sources.

#### V154 `a6cc02d6` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Color is not a single variable but comprises three independent, separately controllable dimensions — hue, saturation, and value (lightness) — and rigorous color research must control all three rather than treating color effects as a function of hue alone. This is the methodological foundation for the claim that brand/color effects depend on saturation and value, not just which hue is chosen."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/341953672_Color_research_in_marketing_Theoretical_and_technical_considerations_for_conducting_rigorous_and_impactful_color_research (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The guidelines include a discussion of the three dimensions of color (hue, saturation, and value) and provide researchers with guidance for controlling these dimensions when creating color stimuli."

*Evidence:* CLAIM CONFIRMED (not refuted). Two legs, both verified.

Leg 1 (color = three controllable dimensions; rigorous research must control all three): Directly supported by the quote and the FULL ABSTRACT of Labrecque (2020), a genuine peer-reviewed article in Psychology & Marketing (Wiley, DOI 10.1002/mar.21359). Abstract: "These guidelines include a discussion of the three dimensions of color (hue, saturation, and value) and provide researchers with guidance for controlling these dimensions when creating color stimuli... for conducting rigorous color research." This is also foundational, century-old colorimetry (Munsell hue/value/chroma; CIE/CIELAB) — not an extraordinary claim requiring extraordinary proof.

Leg 2 (brand effects depend on saturation/value, not just hue): Supported by Labrecque & Milne (2012), J. Academy of Marketing Science (600+ citations), Study 2 — high saturation raises excitement (b=.204, p<.01); high value lowers ruggedness (b=-.344, p<.001).

ADVERSARIAL PROBES (3 found, none fatal): (1) The word "independent" is imprecise — the three dimensions are NOT strictly perceptually independent (Helmholtz-Kohlrausch: saturation/hue alter perceived brightness; Bezold-Bruecke: luminance shifts perceived hue, per Webvision/NIH + Wikipedia). BUT the claim itself glosses "independent" as "separately controllable," which is true of color-space coordinates used to build stimuli, and perceptual non-independence REINFORCES "control all three," it does not refute it. (2) The bare abstract frames controlling all three as forward guidance and does not explicitly indict "hue-alone" prior work; the claim's "rather than hue alone" is a faithful but slightly stronger extrapolation — minor. (3) The DigitalCommons-URI page flags the paper "withdrawn," but a targeted retraction search returned no retraction; that is the institutional repository removing its self-archived PDF (routine publisher-policy practice), NOT a journal retraction — the only retracted item in results was an unrelated community-health color paper. Source is current (2020; the group still published color work in Journal of Marketing in 2025) and its strength exceeds the claim's modest strength.
*Counter-source:* Color-vision literature on perceptual non-independence (Helmholtz-Kohlrausch effect, Bezold-Bruecke shift — Webvision/NIH NCBI Bookshelf NBK11538, Wikipedia) qualifies the word "independent" but does not refute the methodological claim; it strengthens "control all three." No source disputes that color has three controllable dimensions or that brand effects depend on saturation/value.

This directly establishes colour-product congruence as the operative mechanism."

(3) Experiment 1 directly manipulated product type (functional vs sensory-social) x colour type and measured perceived logo APPROPRIATENESS, finding congruent pairings rated more appropriate — exactly what the claim states; "directly establishes... the operative mechanism" is scoped to appropriateness, which is precisely the DV the experiment manipulated, so it is not an overreach. (4) Source quality is sufficient and current: this is the canonical, foundational citation for colour-product congruence, corroborated by later peer-reviewed work (Review of Managerial Science 2018 "One color fits all"; ScienceDirect 2017 "Evoking premiumness: how color-product congruency influences premium evaluations"; Psychology & Marketing reviews). (5) No genuine contradiction: the moderate-incongruity effect and the "incongruence evokes premiumness for utilitarian products" finding apply to PREFERENCE/PREMIUMNESS/favorability outcomes, not to APPROPRIATENESS judgments, so they qualify downstream use but do not refute the appropriateness-congruity finding the claim makes. Not outdated, not marketing fluff, not cherry-picked — peer-reviewed controlled experiment.
*Counter-source:* Qualifier (does NOT refute): the moderate-incongruity effect and Hagtvedt/related "Evoking premiumness" work (ScienceDirect S095032931730246X, 2017) show incongruence can be preferred for some downstream outcomes (premiumness, favorability) for utilitarian products. These concern preference, not appropriateness, so the synthesis should scope the congruence principle to appropriateness/fit and not over-extend it to "congruent colour is always preferred." Also note "operative mechanism" is the single mildly strong phrase; safe to soften to "operative mechanism for perceived appropriateness."

#### V161 `a8605106` — **upheld** · confidence: high

It examined "the relationship between color differentiation and brand performance" using brand-equity scores across 281 brands / 15 categories / 40 subcategories, and concludes: "while color differentiation is helpful for some product categories, it can also be harmful for others." The claim's phrase "harms brand performance" is the paper's own wording. The paper explicitly poses and qualifies the folklore ("is value always gained through differentiation…? whether visual differentiation is always helpful"), so the "refutes the folklore that a distinctive color always benefits the brand" framing is faithful — and logically, the demonstrated harmful categories refute a universal "always" claim. Source quality (peer-reviewed Springer marketing-science journal; Labrecque is an established color-in-marketing scholar) matches the modest claim strength. No contradicting literature found; the 2020 follow-up "One color fits all" (Review of Managerial Science) extends rather than refutes it; distinctive-asset literature (Romaniuk/Sharp) concerns distinctiveness-from-competitors, a different construct. Not outdated. Only caveat: the study is correlational/exploratory and the help/harm split is moderated by dominant-market-leader status and high involvement (norm-adherence helps there) — but the claim is appropriately hedged and direction-neutral, staying within the evidence.

*Claim under review:* "Color is not a single variable but comprises three independent, separately controllable dimensions — hue, saturation, and value (lightness) — and rigorous color research must control all three rather than treating color effects as a function of hue alone. This is the methodological foundation for the claim that brand/color effects depend on saturation and value, not just which hue is chosen."

**Supporting quote:** "The guidelines include a discussion of the three dimensions of color (hue, saturation, and value) and provide researchers with guidance for controlling these dimensions when creating color stimuli."

*Evidence:* All three refutation attempts failed; the claim is well-supported and current. (1) The cited source — Labrecque, "Color research in marketing," Psychology & Marketing 2020 (Wiley, peer-reviewed, DOI 10.1002/mar.21359) — explicitly states "the three dimensions of color (hue, saturation, and value)" and provides "guidance for controlling these dimensions when creating color stimuli," directly matching the claim. The ResearchGate/digitalcommons copies are mirrors; the "withdrawn" note applies only to the institutional self-archived copy, NOT a retraction of the journal article (no retraction found). (2) The substantive half ("effects depend on saturation and value, not just hue") is independently and more strongly confirmed by Labrecque & Milne 2012, J. Academy of Marketing Science (600+ cites): Study 2 measured saturation and value effects on brand personality — high saturation raised excitement (beta=.204, p<.01), high value reduced ruggedness (beta=-.344, p<.001). (3) Standard methodological practice corroborates "control all three": studies "standardize saturation and value" across hues to avoid confounds. (4) The three-dimension HSV/Munsell model is foundational, stable color science — not an extraordinary claim. The only imprecision is the word "independent": the Helmholtz-Kohlrausch effect shows the dimensions are NOT perceptually orthogonal — but the claim uses "independent, separately controllable" in the color-specification sense (true), and that perceptual interaction is itself the reason all three must be controlled, reinforcing rather than refuting the thesis.
*Counter-source:* Helmholtz-Kohlrausch effect (color science): hue, saturation, and value are NOT perceptually independent — saturation raises perceived brightness, hue-dependently (en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helmholtz-Kohlrausch_effect; Color Research & Application, Li 2026). This qualifies the word "independent" but does not refute the claim, since it reinforces the need to control all three dimensions. No source contradicts the core methodological or substantive claim.

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source (Zhou, Xiao, Yoon & Zhu, "The Color of Status," Journal of Consumer Research, Vol. 52, Issue 6, April 2026, p.1232; 7 studies). Every component of the claim checks out: (1) The saturation->status effect mediated by perceived "continuity heritage" via a passage-of-time association is the paper's central finding (matches the supporting quote exactly). (3) The "referential not embodied" classification is the paper's OWN language, not an overlay: the authors explicitly write "The referential meaning of color is context-dependent," frame the association as developing "through repeated exposure," and call them "learned color associations." But the paper's own theoretical label for the resulting color meaning is "referential / context-dependent," so the headline classification stands. Source quality (top-tier JCR, primary), currency (2026), and claim strength all match.
*Counter-source:* Minor precision caveat from the same source: the paper attributes the desaturation<->passage-of-time link to FOUR mechanisms, one being photodegradation (UV/sun fading — a universal physical/ecological process, not a cultural convention). This makes "culturally learned" an incomplete gloss; "learned (partly ecological via fading, partly cultural via media/marketing convention)" is more accurate. Does not invert the claim's referential-not-embodied thesis, which the paper states in its own words ("the referential meaning of color is context-dependent").

#### V166 `a8f3b937` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Across 12 food/beverage brand logos, a statistically significant 'memory color effect' (perceived colour shifting toward the brand's actual colour, measured via achromatic adjustment in DKL colour space) appeared ONLY for high-familiarity logos, not for middle- or low-familiarity ones (ANOVA F(2,26)=8.74, p<.01, ηp²=0.40; high-familiarity shift t(13)=4.24, p<.01). This is direct experimental evidence that brand-colour associations are learned/referential and strengthen with brand exposure, rather than being innate/embodied."

**Source:** https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0068474 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Results demonstrate that the memory color effect occurs only in the high-familiarity condition, but not in the middle- and low-familiarity conditions."

*Evidence:* Verified verbatim against the primary source (PLoS ONE 2013, Kimura et al., "Memory Color Effect Induced by Familiarity of Brand Logos," DOI 10.1371/journal.pone.0068474; PMC3707833). All statistics match exactly: ANOVA F(2,26)=8.74, p<.01, ηp²=0.40; high-familiarity shift t(13)=4.24, p<.01; 12 food/beverage logos (4 high/4 middle/4 low); DKL color space via achromatic adjustment. The supporting quote is verbatim. The interpretation ("learned/referential, strengthens with exposure") is the AUTHORS' OWN: they attribute the effect to "elaboration of the typical color of the object by the repeated exposure," report a positive correlation between effect size and the brand's real store count (r=.38, p<.05 — a direct exposure→strength link), and Exp 2 (semantic priming) shows the brand↔color binding is a learned memory association present only for correctly-colored high-familiarity logos. Source is peer-reviewed primary; not outdated, not marketing, not cherry-picked (controlled two-experiment psychophysics). No retraction or specific dispute of this study found. Non-fatal caveats for the merge: (a) small N=14, normal for within-subjects psychophysics and offset by large ηp²=0.40; (b) the study evidences learned BRAND-SPECIFIC color bindings (Starbucks=green), NOT the broader Labrecque embodied/referential debate about color MEANING (red=exciting) — the claim text correctly limits itself to "brand-colour associations"; (c) the wider cognitive-penetration-of-perception literature has replication critiques (Firestone & Scholl 2016), but that concerns perceptual-vs-post-perceptual locus, not the learned/exposure-dependent conclusion the claim makes.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. General caveat only: top-down-perception/cognitive-penetration replication critiques (Firestone & Scholl 2016, BBS) concern the perceptual locus of such effects, not the learned-association conclusion. Labrecque, Patrick & Milne (2013) embodied/referential framework is about color MEANING, a different construct than the brand-specific color bindings this study tests.

#### V167 `a902d638` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The saturation-to-status effect operates through a LEARNED/referential association, not an innate/embodied one: desaturated colour is associated with the passage of time, which cues perceptions of 'continuity heritage' (mediation confirmed in Study 3). This is a concrete instance of referential (culturally learned) rather than embodied colour meaning."

**Source:** https://academic.oup.com/jcr/advance-article/doi/10.1093/jcr/ucaf029/8120421 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "This effect arises from consumers' association between less saturated colors and the passage of time, leading to perceptions of the brand as having a rich continuity heritage."

*Evidence:* CONFIRMED. Primary source verified: Zhou, Xiao, Yoon & Zhu, "The Color of Status: Color Saturation, Brand Heritage, and Perceived Status of Luxury Brands," Journal of Consumer Research 52(6):1232 (advance article ucaf029) — top-tier peer-reviewed, 2025/2026 (current), 7 experimental/field studies. (A) Empirical core fully supported: low (vs high) saturation -> perceived brand STATUS, MEDIATED by perceived continuity heritage, via the passage-of-time association — and the published abstract/design explicitly state "Study 3 tested the mediation of perceived continuity heritage," so the claim's "mediation confirmed in Study 3" is exactly correct. (B) The interpretive classification is the PAPER'S OWN framing, not an external overlay: the abstract opens "Building on the framework of LEARNED color associations," and the theory section states "the REFERENTIAL meaning of color is context-dependent." The paper never uses "embodied," "innate," or "universal." Per Labrecque, Patrick & Milne (2013) definitions, embodied = "meaning emanating from properties of the color itself" (e.g. short-wavelength blue relaxes) while referential = "context-dependent meaning of colors that is learnt by individuals over time" (e.g. green=eco). A learned, context-dependent, REVERSIBLE association (the effect reverses under innovative positioning, Study 5) is referential by definition, not embodied — so "referential, not embodied" is correct on both the paper's wording and the framework. My initial adversarial angle (universal 'fading=aging' implies embodied/ecological grounding) does NOT survive: Labrecque's "embodied" is narrowly the color property itself, not any universal experience, and the paper classifies this as a learned/referential association regardless of source. CAVEAT (refinement, not refutation): the claim's parenthetical "(culturally learned)" over-narrows the mechanism. The paper grounds the desaturation-time link in MULTIPLE reinforcing routes — natural UV photodegradation/fading (a universal physical regularity), faded old photos/film (a media/era artifact), distant-memory desaturation, and age-targeted marketing convention. So "learned/referential (multiply reinforced)" is precise; "culturally learned" is a slight over-specification of one word. The skill should soften that word but keep the claim. No contradicting source found; nearby Ketron (2025, Psychology & Marketing) saturation work targets a different DV (sensory intensity) and does not conflict.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Adjudicating reference: Labrecque, Patrick & Milne (2013), "The Marketers' Prismatic Palette," Psychology & Marketing 30(2):187-202 — its embodied/referential definitions confirm the classification rather than refute it. Only nuance: the JCR paper (ucaf029) grounds the association in multiple routes including a universal physical one (UV fading), so the claim's "culturally learned" gloss should be softened to "learned/referential (multiply reinforced)."

#### V168 `a923a2c8` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "The congruency effect REVERSES by product type: for utilitarian (functional/health-framed) products, colour-message INCONGRUENCY raises perceived premiumness — e.g. green on a taste-marketed product or red on a health-marketed product is rated more premium. This is the study's falsifiable, counterintuitive core finding and directly qualifies any blanket 'congruent colour is always better' rule."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S095032931730246X (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "When a product is primarily utilitarian, it will be perceived as more premium when the framing of the product and the color are incongruent"

*Evidence:* SUPPORTED by the primary source, with bounding caveats. Source confirmed: Lyons, S.J. & Wien, A.H. (2017/2018), "Evoking premiumness: How color-product congruency influences premium evaluations," Food Quality and Preference 64:103-110 — peer-reviewed Elsevier journal, ~68 Google Scholar citations. The abstract and the verbatim supporting quote directly back the claim: Study 1 (HEDONIC products) found congruent color-framing (red+"delicious", green+"healthy") raised premium perception; Study 2 (UTILITARIAN products) found INCONGRUENT pairings (green+"delicious", red+"healthy") raised premium perception. The reversal-by-product-type is genuinely the study's stated counterintuitive core finding, and the claim's examples are accurate. It does legitimately qualify a blanket "congruent colour is always better" rule for the premium-perception DV.

Adversarial checks did not falsify it, but four caveats bound its weight for codification: (1) Single un-replicated 2018 FOOD-SCIENCE (not marketing) study; each direction rests on ONE experiment, and the utilitarian->incongruency half runs AGAINST the study's own invoked processing-fluency mechanism, leaning on the contested "moderate incongruity effect." (2) DV is narrow — perceived PREMIUMNESS of FOOD PACKAGING under explicit verbal taste/health framing — NOT brand/logo color, not liking/recall; generalizing to the branding-skill's logo-color domain is not supported by this study. (3) A sibling peer-reviewed study (PMC10832432, 2024: packaging color x food type -> purchase intention via perceived fluency) finds CONGRUENCY wins for virtue/utilitarian foods (cold colors), so the incongruency-for-utilitarian result is DV-specific to premiumness, not a general law. (4) The claim's parenthetical "(functional/health-framed)" misreads the design — product TYPE (utilitarian vs hedonic) is orthogonal to message FRAMING (health vs taste); the claim's own taste-marketed example contradicts its own parenthetical.

Verdict: claim faithfully reports a real, current, primary-source finding and is appropriately hedged. Not refuted. For the skill it should be labeled a SINGLE-STUDY finding scoped to food-packaging premium perception (CONVENTION/qualifier strength), NOT a [STRONG] general branding-color law, and the "(health-framed)" gloss should be dropped.
*Counter-source:* https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC10832432/ (Influence of food packaging color and food type on consumer purchase intention: the mediating role of perceived fluency, 2024 — finds CONGRUENCY, not incongruency, optimal for virtue/utilitarian foods on purchase intention; shows the Lyons & Wien reversal is DV-specific to premiumness)

Honest, non-refuting caveats for the synthesis: single study, small N (14), Japanese market, 2013, not independently replicated for the brand-logo case — so label as solid single-study experimental evidence, not a meta-analytic [STRONG]. Source quality (primary, peer-reviewed) matches the claim's strength; no overreach detected.
